0:0	N:N	N:N	In a certain mill lived an old miller who had neither wife nor child, and three apprentices served under him.
1:1	N:N	N:N	As they had been with him several years, he one day said to them, "I am old, and want to sit in the chimney-corner, go out, and whichsoever of you brings me the best horse home, to him will I give the mill, and in return for it he shall take care of me till my death."
2:2	N:D	N:D	The third of the boys was, however, the drudge, who was looked on as foolish by the others; they begrudged the mill to him, and afterwards he would not have it.
3:3	N:N	N:D	Then all three went out together, and when they came to the village, the two said to stupid Hans, "Thou mayst just as well stay here, as long as thou livest thou wilt never get a horse."
4:4	N:N	N:N	Hans, however, went with them, and when it was night they came to a cave in which they lay down to sleep.
5:5	N:N	N:-	The two sharp ones waited until Hans had fallen asleep, then they got up, and went away leaving him where he was.
6:6	N:N	N:N	And they thought they had done a very clever thing, but it was certain to turn out ill for them.
7:7	N:N	N:N	When the sun arose, and Hans woke up, he was lying in a deep cavern.
8:8	Su-:Su-	-:-	He looked around on every side and exclaimed, "Oh, heavens, where am I?"
9:9	Sa:Sa	S:S	Then he got up and clambered out of the cave, went into the forest, and thought, "Here I am quite alone and deserted, how shall I obtain a horse now?"
10:10	N:H	N:H	Whilst he was thus walking full of thought, he met a small tabby-cat which said quite kindly, "Hans, where are you going?"
11:11	Sa:Sa	S:S	"Alas, thou canst not help me."
12:12	N:N	N:N	"I well know your desire," said the cat.
13:13	N:N	N:N	"You wish to have a beautiful horse."
14:14	N:N	N:N	Come with me, and be my faithful servant for seven years long, and then I will give you one more beautiful than any you have ever seen in your whole life."
15:15	Su+:N	+:H	"Well, this is a wonderful cat!" thought Hans, "but I am determined to see if she is telling the truth."
16:16	N:N	N:N	So she took him with her into her enchanted castle, where there were nothing but cats who were her servants.
17:17	H:H	H:H	They leapt nimbly upstairs and downstairs, and were merry and happy.
18:18	N:N	N:H	In the evening when they sat down to dinner, three of them had to make music.
19:19	N:N	N:H	One played the bassoon, the other the fiddle, and the third put the trumpet to his lips, and blew out his cheeks as much as he possibly could.
20:20	N:N	N:H	When they had dined, the table was carried away, and the cat said, "Now, Hans, come and dance with me."
21:21	N:N	N:A	"No," said he, "I won"t dance with a pussy cat.
22:22	N:N	N:N	I have never done that yet."
23:23	N:N	N:N	"Then take him to bed," said she to the cats.
24:24	N:N	N:N	So one of them lighted him to his bed-room, one pulled his shoes off, one his stockings, and at last one of them blew out the candle.
25:25	N:N	N:H	Next morning they returned and helped him out of bed, one put his stockings on for him, one tied his garters, one brought his shoes, one washed him, and one dried his face with her tail.
26:26	H:N	H:H	"That feels very soft!" said Hans.
27:27	N:N	N:N	He, however, had to serve the cat, and chop some wood every day, and to do that, he had an axe of silver, and the wedge and saw were of silver and the mallet of copper.
28:28	N:N	N:N	So he chopped the wood small; stayed there in the house and had good meat and drink, but never saw anyone but the tabby-cat and her servants.
29:29	N:N	N:N	Once she said to him, "Go and mow my meadow, and dry the grass," and gave him a scythe of silver, and a whetstone of gold, but bade him deliver them up again carefully.
30:30	N:N	N:N	So Hans went thither, and did what he was bidden, and when he had finished the work, he carried the scythe, whetstone, and hay to the house, and asked if it was not yet time for her to give him his reward.
31:31	N:N	N:N	"No," said the cat, "you must first do something more for me of the same kind.
32:32	N:N	N:N	There is timber of silver, carpenter"s axe, square, and everything that is needful, all of silver, with these build me a small house."
33:33	N:N	N:S	Then Hans built the small house, and said that he had now done everything, and still he had no horse.
34:34	N:N	N:N	Nevertheless the seven years had gone by with him as if they were six months.
35:35	N:N	N:N	The cat asked him if he would like to see her horses?
36:36	H:N	H:N	"Yes," said Hans.
37:37	H:H	H:H	Then she opened the door of the small house, and when she had opened it, there stood twelve horses, such horses, so bright and shining, that his heart rejoiced at the sight of them.
38:38	N:N	N:H	And now she gave him to eat and drink, and said, "Go home, I will not give thee thy horse away with thee; but in three days" time I will follow thee and bring it."
39:39	N:N	N:N	So Hans set out, and she showed him the way to the mill.
40:40	N:N	N:S	She had, however, never once given him a new coat, and he had been obliged to keep on his dirty old smock-frock, which he had brought with him, and which during the seven years had everywhere become too small for him.
41:41	N:N	N:S	When he reached home, the two other apprentices were there again as well, and each of them certainly had brought a horse with him, but one of them was a blind one, and the other lame.
42:42	N:N	N:N	They asked Hans where his horse was.
43:43	N:N	N:N	"It will follow me in three days" time."
44:44	D:D	D:D	Then they laughed and said, "Indeed, stupid Hans, where wilt thou get a horse?"
45:45	A:N	A:A	"It will be a fine one!"
46:46	D:D	D:D	Hans went into the parlour, but the miller said he should not sit down to table, for he was so ragged and torn, that they would all be ashamed of him if any one came in.
47:47	N:N	N:S	So they gave him a mouthful of food outside, and at night, when they went to rest, the two others would not let him have a bed, and at last he was forced to creep into the goose-house, and lie down on a little hard straw.
48:48	H:H	H:H	In the morning when he awoke, the three days had passed, and a coach came with six horses and they shone so bright that it was delightful to see them! and a servant brought a seventh as well, which was for the poor miller"s boy.
49:49	Su+:N	+:+	And a magnificent princess alighted from the coach and went into the mill, and this princess was the little tabby-cat whom poor Hans had served for seven years.
50:50	N:N	N:+	She asked the miller where the miller"s boy and drudge was?
51:51	N:N	N:N	Then the miller said, "We cannot have him here in the mill, for he is so ragged; he is lying in the goose-house."
52:52	N:N	N:N	Then the King"s daughter said that they were to bring him immediately.
53:53	N:N	N:N	So they brought him out, and he had to hold his little smock-frock together to cover himself.
54:54	N:N	H:H	The servants unpacked splendid garments, and washed him and dressed him, and when that was done, no King could have looked more handsome.
55:55	N:N	N:H	Then the maiden desired to see the horses which the other apprentices had brought home with them, and one of them was blind and the other lame.
56:56	Su+:Su+	+:+	So she ordered the servant to bring the seventh horse, and when the miller saw it, he said that such a horse as that had never yet entered his yard.
57:57	N:N	H:H	"And that is for the third miller"s boy," said she.
58:58	N:N	N:H	"Then he must have the mill," said the miller, but the King"s daughter said that the horse was there, and that he was to keep his mill as well, and took her faithful Hans and set him in the coach, and drove away with him.
59:59	H:N	H:H	They first drove to the little house which he had built with the silver tools, and behold it was a great castle, and everything inside it was of silver and gold; and then she married him, and he was rich, so rich that he had enough for all the rest of his life.
60:60	N:N	H:H	After this, let no one ever say that anyone who is silly can never become a person of importance.
0:0	N:H	N:H	There was once on a time a princess who was extremely proud.
1:1	N:D	N:D	If a wooer came she gave him some riddle to guess, and if he could not find it out, he was sent contemptuously away.
2:2	N:N	N:N	She let it be made known also that whosoever solved her riddle should marry her, let him be who he might.
3:3	N:N	N:N	At length, therefore, three tailors fell in with each other, the two eldest of whom thought they had done so many dexterous bits of work successfully that they could not fail to succeed in this also; the third was a little useless land-louper, who did not even know his trade, but thought he must have some luck in this venture, for where else was it to come from?
4:4	D:N	D:D	Then the two others said to him, "Just stay at home; thou canst not do much with thy little bit of understanding."
5:5	N:H	N:H	The little tailor, however, did not let himself be discouraged, and said he had set his head to work about this for once, and he would manage well enough, and he went forth as if the whole world were his.
6:6	N:N	N:N	They all three announced themselves to the princess, and said she was to propound her riddle to them, and that the right persons were now come, who had understandings so fine that they could be threaded in a needle.
7:7	N:N	N:N	Then said the princess, "I have two kinds of hair on my head, of what color is it?"
8:8	N:N	N:N	"If that be all," said the first, "it must be black and white, like the cloth which is called pepper and salt."
9:9	N:N	N:N	The princess said, "Wrongly guessed; let the second answer."
10:10	N:N	N:N	Then said the second, "If it be not black and white, then it is brown and red, like my father"s company coat."
11:11	N:N	N:N	"Wrongly guessed," said the princess, "let the third give the answer, for I see very well he knows it for certain."
12:12	N:H	N:H	Then the little tailor stepped boldly forth and said, "The princess has a silver and a golden hair on her head, and those are the two different colors."
13:13	Su-:F	-:F	When the princess heard that, she turned pale and nearly fell down with terror, for the little tailor had guessed her riddle, and she had firmly believed that no man on earth could discover it.
14:14	N:N	N:N	When her courage returned she said, "Thou hast not won me yet by that; there is still something else that thou must do.
15:15	N:N	N:N	Below, in the stable is a bear with which thou shalt pass the night, and when I get up in the morning if thou art still alive, thou shalt marry me."
16:16	N:N	N:S	She expected, however, she should thus get rid of the tailor, for the bear had never yet left any one alive who had fallen into his clutches.
17:17	H:H	H:H	The little tailor did not let himself be frightened away, but was quite delighted, and said, "Boldly ventured is half won."
18:18	N:N	N:N	When therefore the evening came, our little tailor was taken down to the bear.
19:19	N:A	N:A	The bear was about to set at the little fellow at once, and give him a hearty welcome with his paws: "Softly, softly," said the little tailor, "I will soon make thee quiet."
20:20	H:H	N:H	Then quite composedly, and as if he had not an anxiety in the world, he took some nuts out of his pocket, cracked them, and ate the kernels.
21:21	H:Sa	H:S	When the bear saw that, he was seized with a desire to have some nuts too.
22:22	N:N	N:N	The tailor felt in his pockets, and reached him a handful; they were, however, not nuts, but pebbles.
23:23	A:N	A:N	The bear put them in his mouth, but could get nothing out of them, let him bite as he would.
24:24	D:D	D:D	"Eh!" thought he, "what a stupid blockhead I am!
25:25	D:N	D:D	I cannot even crack a nut!" and then he said to the tailor, "Here, crack me the nuts."
26:26	N:N	N:D	"There, see what a stupid fellow thou art!" said the little tailor, "to have such a great mouth, and not be able to crack a small nut!"
27:27	N:N	H:N	Then he took the pebble and nimbly put a nut in his mouth in the place of it, and crack, it was in two!
28:28	N:N	N:N	"I must try the thing again," said the bear; "when I watch you, I then think I ought to be able to do it too."
29:29	N:N	N:N	So the tailor once more gave him a pebble, and the bear tried and tried to bite into it with all the strength of his body.
30:30	N:N	N:N	But no one will imagine that he accomplished it.
31:31	N:N	N:N	When that was over, the tailor took out a violin from beneath his coat, and played a piece of it to himself.
32:32	H:H	H:H	When the bear heard the music, he could not help beginning to dance, and when he had danced a while, the thing pleased him so well that he said to the little tailor, "Hark you, is the fiddle heavy?"
33:33	N:N	N:N	"Light enough for a child.
34:34	N:N	H:H	Look, with the left hand I lay my fingers on it, and with the right I stroke it with the bow, and then it goes merrily, hop sa sa vivallalera!"
35:35	H:N	H:N	"So," said the bear; "fiddling is a thing I should like to understand too, that I might dance whenever I had a fancy.
36:36	N:N	N:N	What dost thou think of that?
37:37	N:N	N:N	"Wilt thou give me lessons?"
38:38	H:N	H:N	"With all my heart," said the tailor, "if thou hast a talent for it.
39:39	N:N	N:N	But just let me see thy claws, they are terribly long, I must cut thy nails a little."
40:40	A:A	A:A	Then a vise was brought, and the bear put his claws in it, and the little tailor screwed it tight, and said, "Now wait until I come with the scissors," and he let the bear growl as he liked, and lay down in the corner on a bundle of straw, and fell asleep.
41:41	N:N	N:N	When the princess heard the bear growling so fiercely during the night, she believed nothing else but that he was growling for joy, and had made an end of the tailor.
42:42	Su-:H	-:H	In the morning she arose careless and happy, but when she peeped into the stable, the tailor stood gaily before her, and was as healthy as a fish in water.
43:43	N:N	N:H	Now she could not say another word against the wedding because she had given a promise before every one, and the King ordered a carriage to be brought in which she was to drive to church with the tailor, and there she was to be married.
44:44	N:A	N:A	When they had got into the carriage, the two other tailors, who had false hearts and envied him his good fortune, went into the stable and unscrewed the bear again.
45:45	A:A	A:A	The bear in great fury ran after the carriage.
46:46	F:F	F:F	The princess heard him snorting and growling; she was terrified, and she cried, "Ah, the bear is behind us and wants to get thee!"
47:47	N:N	N:N	The tailor was quick and stood on his head, stuck his legs out of the window, and cried, "Dost thou see the vise?
48:48	N:N	N:N	If thou dost not be off thou shalt be put into it again."
49:49	F:N	F:F	When the bear saw that, he turned round and ran away.
50:50	H:H	H:H	The tailor drove quietly to church, and the princess was married to him at once, and he lived with her as happy as a woodlark.
51:51	N:N	N:N	Whosoever does not believe this, must pay a thaler.
0:0	Sa:N	S:-	Little brother took his little sister by the hand and said, "Since our mother died we have had no happiness; our step-mother beats us every day, and if we come near her she kicks us away with her foot.
1:1	Sa:N	S:N	Our meals are the hard crusts of bread that are left over; and the little dog under the table is better off, for she often throws it a nice bit.
2:2	Sa:N	S:N	May Heaven pity us.
3:3	Sa:N	S:N	If our mother only knew!
4:4	N:N	S:N	Come, we will go forth together into the wide world."
5:5	Sa:Sa	S:S	They walked the whole day over meadows, fields, and stony places; and when it rained the little sister said, "Heaven and our hearts are weeping together."
6:6	Sa:Sa	S:S	In the evening they came to a large forest, and they were so weary with sorrow and hunger and the long walk, that they lay down in a hollow tree and fell asleep.
7:7	N:N	N:N	The next day when they awoke, the sun was already high in the sky, and shone down hot into the tree.
8:8	N:N	N:N	Then the brother said, "Sister, I am thirsty; if I knew of a little brook I would go and just take a drink; I think I hear one running."
9:9	N:N	N:N	The brother got up and took the little sister by the hand, and they set off to find the brook.
10:10	N:N	N:-	But the wicked step-mother was a witch, and had seen how the two children had gone away, and had crept after them privily, as witches do creep, and had bewitched all the brooks in the forest.
11:11	N:N	N:-	Now when they found a little brook leaping brightly over the stones, the brother was going to drink out of it, but the sister heard how it said as it ran, "Who drinks of me will be a tiger; who drinks of me will be a tiger."
12:12	F:N	F:N	Then the sister cried, "Pray, dear brother, do not drink, or you will become a wild beast, and tear me to pieces."
13:13	N:N	N:N	The brother did not drink, although he was so thirsty, but said, "I will wait for the next spring."
14:14	N:N	N:-	When they came to the next brook the sister heard this also say, "Who drinks of me will be a wolf; who drinks of me will be a wolf."
15:15	F:N	F:-	Then the sister cried out, "Pray, dear brother, do not drink, or you will become a wolf, and devour me."
16:16	A:N	N:-	The brother did not drink, and said, "I will wait until we come to the next spring, but then I must drink, say what you like; for my thirst is too great."
17:17	N:N	N:-	And when they came to the third brook the sister heard how it said as it ran, "Who drinks of me will be a roebuck; who drinks of me will be a roebuck."
18:18	F:N	F:-	The sister said, "Oh, I pray you, dear brother, do not drink, or you will become a roebuck, and run away from me."
19:19	N:N	-:-	But the brother had knelt down at once by the brook, and had bent down and drunk some of the water, and as soon as the first drops touched his lips he lay there a young roebuck.
20:20	Sa:Sa	S:S	And now the sister wept over her poor bewitched brother, and the little roe wept also, and sat sorrowfully near to her.
21:21	N:N	S:-	But at last the girl said, "Be quiet, dear little roe, I will never, never leave you."
22:22	N:N	N:N	Then she untied her golden garter and put it round the roebuck"s neck, and she plucked rushes and wove them into a soft cord.
23:23	N:N	N:N	With this she tied the little beast and led it on, and she walked deeper and deeper into the forest.
24:24	N:N	N:N	And when they had gone a very long way they came at last to a little house, and the girl looked in; and as it was empty, she thought, "We can stay here and live."
25:25	H:N	H:N	Then she sought for leaves and moss to make a soft bed for the roe; and every morning she went out and gathered roots and berries and nuts for herself, and brought tender grass for the roe, who ate out of her hand, and was content and played round about her.
26:26	N:N	H:N	In the evening, when the sister was tired, and had said her prayer, she laid her head upon the roebuck"s back: that was her pillow, and she slept softly on it.
27:27	N:N	H:N	And if only the brother had had his human form it would have been a delightful life.
28:28	N:N	N:N	For some time they were alone like this in the wilderness.
29:29	N:N	N:N	But it happened that the King of the country held a great hunt in the forest.
30:30	Su+:N	+:N	Then the blasts of the horns, the barking of dogs, and the merry shouts of the huntsmen rang through the trees, and the roebuck heard all, and was only too anxious to be there.
31:31	N:Sa	+:N	"Oh," said he, to his sister, "let me be off to the hunt, I cannot bear it any longer;" and he begged so much that at last she agreed.
32:32	N:N	N:N	"But," said she to him, "come back to me in the evening; I must shut my door for fear of the rough huntsmen, so knock and say, "My little sister, let me in!" that I may know you; and if you do not say that, I shall not open the door."
33:33	H:H	H:N	Then the young roebuck sprang away; so happy was he and so merry in the open air.
34:34	N:N	N:N	The King and the huntsmen saw the pretty creature, and started after him, but they could not catch him, and when they thought that they surely had him, away he sprang through the bushes and could not be seen.
35:35	N:N	N:N	When it was dark he ran to the cottage, knocked, and said, "My little sister, let me in."
36:36	N:N	N:N	Then the door was opened for him, and he jumped in, and rested himself the whole night through upon his soft bed.
37:37	H:N	H:N	The next day the hunt went on afresh, and when the roebuck again heard the bugle-horn, and the ho! ho! of the huntsmen, he had no peace, but said, "Sister, let me out, I must be off."
38:38	N:N	N:N	His sister opened the door for him, and said, "But you must be here again in the evening and say your pass-word."
39:39	N:N	N:N	When the King and his huntsmen again saw the young roebuck with the golden collar, they all chased him, but he was too quick and nimble for them.
40:40	N:N	N:-	This went on for the whole day, but at last by the evening the huntsmen had surrounded him, and one of them wounded him a little in the foot, so that he limped and ran slowly.
41:41	N:N	N:N	Then a hunter crept after him to the cottage and heard how he said, "My little sister, let me in," and saw that the door was opened for him, and was shut again at once.
42:42	N:N	N:N	The huntsman took notice of it all, and went to the King and told him what he had seen and heard.
43:43	N:N	N:N	Then the King said, "To-morrow we will hunt once more."
44:44	F:F	F:F	The little sister, however, was dreadfully frightened when she saw that her fawn was hurt.
45:45	N:N	N:N	She washed the blood off him, laid herbs on the wound, and said, "Go to your bed, dear roe, that you may get well again."
46:46	N:N	N:N	But the wound was so slight that the roebuck, next morning, did not feel it any more.
47:47	N:N	N:N	And when he again heard the sport outside, he said, "I cannot bear it, I must be there; they shall not find it so easy to catch me."
48:48	F:Sa	F:S	The sister cried, and said, "This time they will kill you, and here am I alone in the forest and forsaken by all the world.
49:49	F:N	F:S	I will not let you out."
50:50	A:N	A:S	"Then you will have me die of grief," answered the roe; "when I hear the bugle-horns I feel as if I must jump out of my skin."
51:51	F:H	F:S	Then the sister could not do otherwise, but opened the door for him with a heavy heart, and the roebuck, full of health and joy, bounded into the forest.
52:52	N:N	N:N	When the King saw him, he said to his huntsmen, "Now chase him all day long till night-fall, but take care that no one does him any harm."
53:53	N:N	N:N	As soon as the sun had set, the King said to the huntsman, "Now come and show me the cottage in the wood;" and when he was at the door, he knocked and called out, "Dear little sister, let me in."
54:54	Su+:N	+:N	Then the door opened, and the King walked in, and there stood a maiden more lovely than any he had ever seen.
55:55	F:F	F:F	The maiden was frightened when she saw, not her little roe, but a man come in who wore a golden crown upon his head.
56:56	H:N	H:H	But the King looked kindly at her, stretched out his hand, and said, "Will you go with me to my palace and be my dear wife?"
57:57	H:N	H:H	"Yes, indeed," answered the maiden, "but the little roe must go with me, I cannot leave him."
58:58	N:N	H:N	The King said, "It shall stay with you as long as you live, and shall want nothing."
59:59	N:N	H:N	Just then he came running in, and the sister again tied him with the cord of rushes, took it in her own hand, and went away with the King from the cottage.
60:60	N:N	H:N	The King took the lovely maiden upon his horse and carried her to his palace, where the wedding was held with great pomp.
61:61	H:N	H:H	She was now the Queen, and they lived for a long time happily together; the roebuck was tended and cherished, and ran about in the palace-garden.
62:62	N:N	N:-	But the wicked step-mother, because of whom the children had gone out into the world, thought all the time that the sister had been torn to pieces by the wild beasts in the wood, and that the brother had been shot for a roebuck by the huntsmen.
63:63	A:A	A:A	Now when she heard that they were so happy, and so well off, envy and hatred rose in her heart and left her no peace, and she thought of nothing but how she could bring them again to misfortune.
64:64	D:N	D:-	Her own daughter, who was ugly as night, and had only one eye, grumbled at her and said, "A Queen! that ought to have been my luck."
65:65	A:N	A:A	"Only be quiet," answered the old woman, and comforted her by saying, "when the time comes I shall be ready."
66:66	N:N	N:-	As time went on, the Queen had a pretty little boy, and it happened that the King was out hunting; so the old witch took the form of the chamber-maid, went into the room where the Queen lay, and said to her, "Come, the bath is ready; it will do you good, and give you fresh strength; make haste before it gets cold."
67:67	N:N	N:-	The daughter also was close by; so they carried the weakly Queen into the bath-room, and put her into the bath; then they shut the door and ran away.
68:68	N:N	-:-	But in the bath-room they had made a fire of such deadly heat that the beautiful young Queen was soon suffocated.
69:69	N:N	N:-	When this was done the old woman took her daughter, put a nightcap on her head, and laid her in bed in place of the Queen.

0:0	N:N	N:N	Let no one ever say that a poor tailor cannot do great things and win high honors; all that is needed is that he should go to the right smithy, and what is of most consequence, that he should have good luck.
1:1	N:N	N:S	A civil, adroit tailor"s apprentice once went out travelling, and came into a great forest, and, as he did not know the way, he lost himself.
2:2	Sa:Sa	S:S	Night fell, and nothing was left for him to do, but to seek a bed in this painful solitude.

4:4	H:N	N:H	He sought out a high oak, climbed up to the top of it, and thanked God that he had his goose with him, for otherwise the wind which blew over the top of the tree would have carried him away.

10:10	D:A	D:A	"Go your way," replied the old man in a surly voice, "I will have nothing to do with runagates; seek for yourself a shelter elsewhere."

15:15	N:N	N:-	Then close by the hut, he saw a great black bull and a beautiful stag, which were just preparing for a violent struggle.

17:17	N:N	-:A	For a long time it was uncertain which of the two would gain the victory; at length the stag thrust his horns into his adversary"s body, whereupon the bull fell to the earth with a terrific roar, and was thoroughly despatched by a few strokes from the stag.

74:74	F:F	F:H	Then her voice came back to her, and she cried out loudly, "Yes, Mary, I did it;" and straight-way rain fell from the sky and extinguished the flames of fire, and a light broke forth above her, and the Virgin Mary descended with the two little sons by her side, and the new-born daughter in her arms.

0:0	N:N	N:N	Once upon a time, a certain King was hunting in a great forest, and he chased a wild beast so eagerly that none of his attendants could follow him.

49:49	N:N	N:N	So they went to him at once, and threw him down, and his soul broke into fragments, and went into the gambling vagabonds who are living this very day.
0:0	N:Sa	N:S	In olden times, when the Lord himself still used to walk about on this earth amongst men, it once happened that he was tired and overtaken by the darkness before he could reach an inn.
1:1	N:N	N:N	Now there stood on the road before him two houses facing each other; the one large and beautiful, the other small and poor.
2:2	N:N	N:N	The large one belonged to a rich man, and the small one to a poor man.
3:3	N:N	N:N	Then the Lord thought, "I shall be no burden to the rich man, I will stay the night with him."
4:4	N:N	N:N	When the rich man heard some one knocking at his door, he opened the window and asked the stranger what he wanted.
5:5	N:N	N:N	The Lord answered, "I only ask for a night"s lodging."
6:6	D:N	D:D	Then the rich man looked at the traveler from head to foot, and as the Lord was wearing common clothes, and did not look like one who had much money in his pocket, he shook his head, and said, "No, I cannot take you in, my rooms are full of herbs and seeds; and if I were to lodge everyone who knocked at my door, I might very soon go begging myself.
7:7	N:N	N:N	Go somewhere else for a lodging," and with this he shut down the window and left the Lord standing there.
8:8	N:N	N:N	So the Lord turned his back on the rich man, and went across to the small house and knocked.
9:9	N:N	N:N	He had hardly done so when the poor man opened the little door and bade the traveler come in.
10:10	H:N	H:H	"Pass the night with me, it is already dark," said he; "you cannot go any further to-night."
11:11	H:H	H:H	This pleased the Lord, and he went in.
12:12	H:N	H:H	The poor man"s wife shook hands with him, and welcomed him, and said he was to make himself at home and put up with what they had got; they had not much to offer him, but what they had they would give him with all their hearts.
13:13	N:N	N:H	Then she put the potatoes on the fire, and while they were boiling, she milked the goat, that they might have a little milk with them.
14:14	H:H	H:H	When the cloth was laid, the Lord sat down with the man and his wife, and he enjoyed their coarse food, for there were happy faces at the table.
15:15	N:N	N:H	When they had had supper and it was bed-time, the woman called her husband apart and said, "Hark you, dear husband, let us make up a bed of straw for ourselves to-night, and then the poor traveler can sleep in our bed and have a good rest, for he has been walking the whole day through, and that makes one weary."
16:16	H:N	H:N	"With all my heart," he answered, "I will go and offer it to him;" and he went to the stranger and invited him, if he had no objection, to sleep in their bed and rest his limbs properly.
17:17	N:N	N:H	But the Lord was unwilling to take their bed from the two old folks; however, they would not be satisfied, until at length he did it and lay down in their bed, while they themselves lay on some straw on the ground.
18:18	N:N	N:H	Next morning they got up before daybreak, and made as good a breakfast as they could for the guest.
19:19	N:N	N:H	When the sun shone in through the little window, and the Lord had got up, he again ate with them, and then prepared to set out on his journey.
20:20	N:N	N:H	But as he was standing at the door he turned round and said, "As you are so kind and good, you may wish three things for yourselves and I will grant them."
21:21	H:N	H:H	Then the man said, "What else should I wish for but eternal happiness, and that we two, as long as we live, may be healthy and have every day our daily bread; for the third wish, I do not know what to have."
22:22	N:N	N:N	And the Lord said to him, "Will you wish for a new house instead of this old one?"
23:23	H:H	H:H	"Oh, yes," said the man; "if I can have that, too, I should like it very much."
24:24	N:N	N:H	And the Lord fulfilled his wish, and changed their old house into a new one, again gave them his blessing, and went on.
25:25	N:N	N:N	The sun was high when the rich man got up and leaned out of his window and saw, on the opposite side of the way, a new clean-looking house with red tiles and bright windows where the old hut used to be.
26:26	Su-:Su-	-:-	He was very much astonished, and called his wife and said to her, "Tell me, what can have happened?
27:27	N:N	-:-	Last night there was a miserable little hut standing there, and to-day there is a beautiful new house.
28:28	N:N	N:-	Run over and see how that has come to pass."
29:29	N:N	N:H	So his wife went and asked the poor man, and he said to her, "Yesterday evening a traveler came here and asked for a night"s lodging, and this morning when he took leave of us he granted us three wishes---eternal happiness, health during this life and our daily bread as well, and besides this, a beautiful new house instead of our old hut."
30:30	N:N	N:N	When the rich man"s wife heard this, she ran back in haste and told her husband how it had happened.
31:31	A:N	A:A	The man said, "I could tear myself to pieces!

33:33	D:N	D:A	That traveler came to our house too, and wanted to sleep here, and I sent him away."

35:35	N:N	N:A	You can still catch the man up, and then you must ask to have three wishes granted to you."
36:36	N:N	N:N	The rich man followed the good counsel and galloped away on his horse, and soon came up with the Lord.
37:37	N:N	N:N	He spoke to him softly and pleasantly, and begged him not to take it amiss that he had not let him in directly; he was looking for the front-door key, and in the meantime the stranger had gone away, if he returned the same way he must come and stay with him.
38:38	N:N	N:N	"Yes," said the Lord; "if I ever come back again, I will do so."

41:41	N:N	N:N	So the Lord said to him, "Ride home, then, and three wishes which you shall form, shall be fulfilled."
42:42	H:N	H:H	The rich man had now gained what he wanted, so he rode home, and began to consider what he should wish for.
43:43	N:N	N:S	As he was thus thinking he let the bridle fall, and the horse began to caper about, so that he was continually disturbed in his meditations, and could not collect his thoughts at all.
44:44	N:N	N:N	He patted its neck, and said, "Gently, Lisa," but the horse only began new tricks.

0:0	N:N	N:N	Once on a time a countryman had a son who was as big as a thumb, and did not become any bigger, and during several years did not grow one hair"s breadth.

0:0	N:N	N:N	There was once upon a time a rich King who had three daughters, who daily went to walk in the palace garden, and the King was a great lover of all kinds of fine trees, but there was one for which he had such an affection, that if anyone gathered an apple from it he wished him a hundred fathoms underground.
1:1	N:N	N:N	And when harvest time came, the apples on this tree were all as red as blood.
2:2	N:N	N:N	The three daughters went every day beneath the tree, and looked to see if the wind had not blown down an apple, but they never by any chance found one, and the tree was so loaded with them that it was almost breaking, and the branches hung down to the ground.
3:3	N:N	N:N	Then the King"s youngest child had a great desire for an apple, and said to her sisters, "Our father loves us far too much to wish us underground, it is my belief that he would only do that to people who were strangers."
4:4	H:H	H:H	And while she was speaking, the child plucked off quite a large apple, and ran to her sisters, saying, "Just taste, my dear little sisters, for never in my life have I tasted anything so delightful."

7:7	N:N	-:S	He sought them everywhere in the palace and garden, but could not find them.

10:10	N:N	N:N	Three young huntsmen also went out, and when they had travelled about for eight days, they arrived at a great castle, in which were beautiful apartments, and in one room a table was laid on which were delicate dishes which were still so warm that they were smoking, but in the whole of the castle no human being was either to be seen or heard.
11:11	N:N	N:N	They waited there for half a day, and the food still remained warm and smoking, and at length they were so hungry that they sat down and ate, and agreed with each other that they would stay and live in that castle, and that one of them, who should be chosen by casting lots, should remain in the house, and the two others seek the King"s daughters.
12:12	N:N	N:N	They cast lots, and the lot fell on the eldest; so next day the two younger went out to seek, and the eldest had to stay home.
13:13	N:N	N:N	At mid-day came a small, small mannikin and begged for a piece of bread, then the huntsman took the bread which he had found there, and cut a round off the loaf and was about to give it to him, but whilst he was giving it to the mannikin, the latter let it fall, and asked the huntsman to be so good as to give him that piece again.

39:39	N:N	N:N	Then first came two white doves in at the kitchen window; next came two turtle-doves; and after them came all the little birds under heaven, chirping and hopping about.
40:40	N:N	N:N	And they flew down into the ashes; and the little doves put their heads down and set to work, pick, pick, pick; and then the others began pick, pick, pick; and they put all the good grain into the dishes, and left all the ashes.
41:41	N:N	N:N	Before half an hour"s time all was done, and out they flew again.

52:52	N:N	N:N	But when they had broken open the door they found no one within; and as they came back into the house, Ashputtel was lying, as she always did, in her dirty frock by the ashes, and her dim little lamp was burning in the chimney.
53:53	N:N	N:N	For she had run as quickly as she could through the pigeon-house and on to the hazel-tree, and had there taken off her beautiful clothes, and put them beneath the tree, that the bird might carry them away, and had lain down again amid the ashes in her little grey frock.
54:54	N:N	N:N	The next day when the feast was again held, and her father, mother, and sisters were gone, Ashputtel went to the hazel-tree, and said:

58:58	N:N	N:N	When night came she wanted to go home; and the king"s son followed here as before, that he might see into what house she went: but she sprang away from him all at once into the garden behind her father"s house.

60:60	N:N	N:N	Then the king"s son lost sight of her, and could not find out where she was gone, but waited till her father came home, and said to him, "The unknown lady who danced with me has slipped away, and I think she must have sprung into the pear-tree."

63:63	N:N	N:N	And when they came back into the kitchen, there lay Ashputtel among the ashes; for she had slipped down on the other side of the tree, and carried her beautiful clothes back to the bird at the hazel-tree, and then put on her little grey frock.
64:64	N:N	N:N	The third day, when her father and mother and sisters were gone, she went again into the garden, and said:

67:67	F:N	F:H	When night came she wanted to go home; and the king"s son would go with her, and said to himself, "I will not lose her this time"; but, however, she again slipped away from him, though in such a hurry that she dropped her left golden slipper upon the stairs.
68:68	N:N	N:N	The prince took the shoe, and went the next day to the king his father, and said, "I will take for my wife the lady that this golden slipper fits."

0:0	N:N	N:N	A king and queen once upon a time reigned in a country a great way off, where there were in those days fairies.
1:1	Sa:Sa	S:S	Now this king and queen had plenty of money, and plenty of fine clothes to wear, and plenty of good things to eat and drink, and a coach to ride out in every day: but though they had been married many years they had no children, and this grieved them very much indeed.
2:2	F:Sa	F:S	But one day as the queen was walking by the side of the river, at the bottom of the garden, she saw a poor little fish, that had thrown itself out of the water, and lay gasping and nearly dead on the bank.
3:3	H:H	H:H	Then the queen took pity on the little fish, and threw it back again into the river; and before it swam away it lifted its head out of the water and said, "I know what your wish is, and it shall be fulfilled, in return for your kindness to me--you will soon have a daughter."
4:4	H:H	H:H	What the little fish had foretold soon came to pass; and the queen had a little girl, so very beautiful that the king could not cease looking on it for joy, and said he would hold a great feast and make merry, and show the child to all the land.
5:5	N:N	N:H	So he asked his kinsmen, and nobles, and friends, and neighbours.
6:6	N:N	N:H	But the queen said, "I will have the fairies also, that they might be kind and good to our little daughter."
7:7	N:N	N:N	Now there were thirteen fairies in the kingdom; but as the king and queen had only twelve golden dishes for them to eat out of, they were forced to leave one of the fairies without asking her.
8:8	N:H	N:H	So twelve fairies came, each with a high red cap on her head, and red shoes with high heels on her feet, and a long white wand in her hand: and after the feast was over they gathered round in a ring and gave all their best gifts to the little princess.
9:9	N:N	N:H	One gave her goodness, another beauty, another riches, and so on till she had all that was good in the world.
10:10	N:A	N:A	Just as eleven of them had done blessing her, a great noise was heard in the courtyard, and word was brought that the thirteenth fairy was come, with a black cap on her head, and black shoes on her feet, and a broomstick in her hand: and presently up she came into the dininghall.
11:11	A:A	A:A	Now, as she had not been asked to the feast she was very angry, and scolded the king and queen very much, and set to work to take her revenge.
12:12	A:A	A:A	So she cried out, "The king"s daughter shall, in her fifteenth year, be wounded by a spindle, and fall down dead."
13:13	N:Sa	N:S	Then the twelfth of the friendly fairies, who had not yet given her gift, came forward, and said that the evil wish must be fulfilled, but that she could soften its mischief; so her gift was, that the king"s daughter, when the spindle wounded her, should not really die, but should only fall asleep for a hundred years.
14:14	F:F	F:F	However, the king hoped still to save his dear child altogether from the threatened evil; so he ordered that all the spindles in the kingdom should be bought up and burnt.
15:15	N:N	N:H	But all the gifts of the first eleven fairies were in the meantime fulfilled; for the princess was so beautiful, and well behaved, and good, and wise, that everyone who knew her loved her.
16:16	N:N	N:N	It happened that, on the very day she was fifteen years old, the king and queen were not at home, and she was left alone in the palace.
17:17	N:N	N:N	So she roved about by herself, and looked at all the rooms and chambers, till at last she came to an old tower, to which there was a narrow staircase ending with a little door.
18:18	N:Su-	N:-	In the door there was a golden key, and when she turned it the door sprang open, and there sat an old lady spinning away very busily.
19:19	N:Su-	N:-	"Why, how now, good mother," said the princess; "what are you doing there?"
20:20	N:N	N:N	"Spinning," said the old lady, and nodded her head, humming a tune, while buzz! went the wheel.
21:21	H:N	H:N	"How prettily that little thing turns round!" said the princess, and took the spindle and began to try and spin.
22:22	N:Sa	N:S	But scarcely had she touched it, before the fairy"s prophecy was fulfilled; the spindle wounded her, and she fell down lifeless on the ground.
23:23	N:Su-	N:S	However, she was not dead, but had only fallen into a deep sleep; and the king and the queen, who had just come home, and all their court, fell asleep too; and the horses slept in the stables, and the dogs in the court, the pigeons on the house-top, and the very flies slept upon the walls.
24:24	N:Su-	N:-	Even the fire on the hearth left off blazing, and went to sleep; the jack stopped, and the spit that was turning about with a goose upon it for the king"s dinner stood still; and the cook, who was at that moment pulling the kitchen-boy by the hair to give him a box on the ear for something he had done amiss, let him go, and both fell asleep; the butler, who was slyly tasting the ale, fell asleep with the jug at his lips: and thus everything stood still, and slept soundly.
25:25	N:N	N:S	A large hedge of thorns soon grew round the palace, and every year it became higher and thicker; till at last the old palace was surrounded and hidden, so that not even the roof or the chimneys could be seen.
26:26	N:N	N:N	But there went a report through all the land of the beautiful sleeping Briar Rose (for so the king"s daughter was called): so that, from time to time, several kings" sons came, and tried to break through the thicket into the palace.
27:27	N:Sa	N:S	This, however, none of them could ever do; for the thorns and bushes laid hold of them, as it were with hands; and there they stuck fast, and died wretchedly.
28:28	N:N	N:S	After many, many years there came a king"s son into that land: and an old man told him the story of the thicket of thorns; and how a beautiful palace stood behind it, and how a wonderful princess, called Briar Rose, lay in it asleep, with all her court.
29:29	N:N	N:S	He told, too, how he had heard from his grandfather that many, many princes had come, and had tried to break through the thicket, but that they had all stuck fast in it, and died.
30:30	N:N	N:N	Then the young prince said, "All this shall not frighten me; I will go and see this Briar Rose."
31:31	N:F	N:F	The old man tried to hinder him, but he was bent upon going.
32:32	N:Su+	H:+	Now that very day the hundred years were ended; and as the prince came to the thicket he saw nothing but beautiful flowering shrubs, through which he went with ease, and they shut in after him as thick as ever.
33:33	N:N	N:N	Then he came at last to the palace, and there in the court lay the dogs asleep; and the horses were standing in the stables; and on the roof sat the pigeons fast asleep, with their heads under their wings.
34:34	N:N	N:N	And when he came into the palace, the flies were sleeping on the walls; the spit was standing still; the butler had the jug of ale at his lips, going to drink a draught; the maid sat with a fowl in her lap ready to be plucked; and the cook in the kitchen was still holding up her hand, as if she was going to beat the boy.
35:35	N:N	N:N	Then he went on still farther, and all was so still that he could hear every breath he drew; till at last he came to the old tower, and opened the door of the little room in which Briar Rose was; and there she lay, fast asleep on a couch by the window.
36:36	H:H	H:H	She looked so beautiful that he could not take his eyes off her, so he stooped down and gave her a kiss.
37:37	H:H	H:H	But the moment he kissed her she opened her eyes and awoke, and smiled upon him; and they went out together; and soon the king and queen also awoke, and all the court, and gazed on each other with great wonder.
38:38	N:Su+	H:+	And the horses shook themselves, and the dogs jumped up and barked; the pigeons took their heads from under their wings, and looked about and flew into the fields; the flies on the walls buzzed again; the fire in the kitchen blazed up; round went the jack, and round went the spit, with the goose for the king"s dinner upon it; the butler finished his draught of ale; the maid went on plucking the fowl; and the cook gave the boy the box on his ear.
39:39	H:H	H:H	And then the prince and Briar Rose were married, and the wedding feast was given; and they lived happily together all their lives long.
0:0	N:N	N:N	There was once a king, whose queen had hair of the purest gold, and was so beautiful that her match was not to be met with on the whole face of the earth.
1:1	Sa:Sa	S:S	But this beautiful queen fell ill, and when she felt that her end drew near she called the king to her and said, "Promise me that you will never marry again, unless you meet with a wife who is as beautiful as I am, and who has golden hair like mine."
2:2	Sa:Sa	S:S	Then when the king in his grief promised all she asked, she shut her eyes and died.
3:3	Sa:Sa	S:S	But the king was not to be comforted, and for a long time never thought of taking another wife.
4:4	N:A	N:S	At last, however, his wise men said, "this will not do; the king must marry again, that we may have a queen."
5:5	N:N	N:N	So messengers were sent far and wide, to seek for a bride as beautiful as the late queen.
6:6	N:N	N:N	But there was no princess in the world so beautiful; and if there had been, still there was not one to be found who had golden hair.
7:7	N:Sa	N:S	So the messengers came home, and had had all their trouble for nothing.
8:8	N:N	N:N	Now the king had a daughter, who was just as beautiful as her mother, and had the same golden hair.
9:9	N:N	N:N	And when she was grown up, the king looked at her and saw that she was just like this late queen: then he said to his courtiers, "May I not marry my daughter?
10:10	N:A	N:A	She is the very image of my dead wife: unless I have her, I shall not find any bride upon the whole earth, and you say there must be a queen."
11:11	Su-:Su-	-:-	When the courtiers heard this they were shocked, and said, "Heaven forbid that a father should marry his daughter!
12:12	Su-:D	-:D	Out of so great a sin no good can come."
13:13	Su-:Su-	-:-	And his daughter was also shocked, but hoped the king would soon give up such thoughts; so she said to him, "Before I marry anyone I must have three dresses: one must be of gold, like the sun; another must be of shining silver, like the moon; and a third must be dazzling as the stars: besides this, I want a mantle of a thousand different kinds of fur put together, to which every beast in the kingdom must give a part of his skin."
14:14	N:N	N:-	And thus she though he would think of the matter no more.
15:15	N:N	N:N	But the king made the most skilful workmen in his kingdom weave the three dresses: one golden, like the sun; another silvery, like the moon; and a third sparkling, like the stars: and his hunters were told to hunt out all the beasts in his kingdom, and to take the finest fur out of their skins: and thus a mantle of a thousand furs was made.
16:16	N:N	N:N	When all were ready, the king sent them to her; but she got up in the night when all were asleep, and took three of her trinkets, a golden ring, a golden necklace, and a golden brooch, and packed the three dresses--of the sun, the moon, and the stars--up in a nutshell, and wrapped herself up in the mantle made of all sorts of fur, and besmeared her face and hands with soot.
17:17	F:F	F:F	Then she threw herself upon Heaven for help in her need, and went away, and journeyed on the whole night, till at last she came to a large wood.
18:18	N:N	N:N	As she was very tired, she sat herself down in the hollow of a tree and soon fell asleep: and there she slept on till it was midday.
19:19	N:N	N:N	Now as the king to whom the wood belonged was hunting in it, his dogs came to the tree, and began to snuff about, and run round and round, and bark.

21:21	Su+:Su+	+:+	And the huntsmen went up to the tree, and when they came back again said, "In the hollow tree there lies a most wonderful beast, such as we never saw before; its skin seems to be of a thousand kinds of fur, but there it lies fast asleep."
22:22	N:H	N:H	"See," said the king, "if you can catch it alive, and we will take it with us."

24:24	N:N	N:N	Then they said, "Yes, Miss Cat-skin, you will do for the kitchen; you can sweep up the ashes, and do things of that sort."
25:25	N:N	N:N	So they put her into the coach, and took her home to the king"s palace.
26:26	N:Sa	N:S	Then they showed her a little corner under the staircase, where no light of day ever peeped in, and said, "Cat-skin, you may lie and sleep there."
27:27	N:N	N:S	And she was sent into the kitchen, and made to fetch wood and water, to blow the fire, pluck the poultry, pick the herbs, sift the ashes, and do all the dirty work.
28:28	Sa:Sa	S:S	Thus Cat-skin lived for a long time very sorrowfully.

39:39	N:N	N:N	The truth was, that she had run into her little cabin, pulled off her dress, blackened her face and hands, put on the fur-skin cloak, and was Catskin again.
40:40	N:A	N:A	When she went into the kitchen to her work, and began to rake the ashes, the cook said, "Let that alone till the morning, and heat the king"s soup; I should like to run up now and give a peep: but take care you don"t let a hair fall into it, or you will run a chance of never eating again."
41:41	N:N	N:N	As soon as the cook went away, Cat-skin heated the king"s soup, and toasted a slice of bread first, as nicely as ever she could; and when it was ready, she went and looked in the cabin for her little golden ring, and put it into the dish in which the soup was.
42:42	H:H	H:H	When the dance was over, the king ordered his soup to be brought in; and it pleased him so well, that he thought he had never tasted any so good before.

46:46	N:N	N:F	"I did," answered the cook.
47:47	D:D	N:D	But the king said, "That is not true; it was better done than you could do it."
48:48	N:N	N:N	Then he answered, "To tell the truth I did not cook it, but Cat-skin did."

54:54	N:N	N:N	Then she would not own that she knew anything about the ring; so the king sent her away again about her business.

56:56	N:N	N:N	"Yes," said he, "but come again in half an hour, and cook the king the soup that he likes so much."

58:58	N:N	N:N	After the dance was at an end she managed to slip out, so slyly that the king did not see where she was gone; but she sprang into her little cabin, and made herself into Cat-skin again, and went into the kitchen to cook the soup.

69:69	N:Su+	H:+	When the king got to the bottom, he ordered Cat-skin to be called once more, and soon saw the white finger, and the ring that he had put on it whilst they were dancing: so he seized her hand, and kept fast hold of it, and when she wanted to loose herself and spring away, the fur cloak fell off a little on one side, and the starry dress sparkled underneath it.
70:70	N:Su+	H:+	Then he got hold of the fur and tore it off, and her golden hair and beautiful form were seen, and she could no longer hide herself: so she washed the soot and ashes from her face, and showed herself to be the most beautiful princess upon the face of the earth.

0:0	N:N	N:N	There was once upon a time a poor peasant called Crabb, who drove with two oxen a load of wood to the town, and sold it to a doctor for two talers.

67:67	N:N	N:N	Frederick of course made no objection to that plan, and they set off into the wood to look for the thieves; but they could not find them: and when it grew dark, they climbed up into a tree to spend the night there.
68:68	N:Su+	N:N	Scarcely were they up, than who should come by but the very rogues they were looking for.
69:69	N:N	N:N	They were in truth great rascals, and belonged to that class of people who find things before they are lost; they were tired; so they sat down and made a fire under the very tree where Frederick and Catherine were.
70:70	N:N	N:N	Frederick slipped down on the other side, and picked up some stones.
71:71	N:Su-	N:N	Then he climbed up again, and tried to hit the thieves on the head with them: but they only said, "It must be near morning, for the wind shakes the fir-apples down."
72:72	N:Sa	N:N	Catherine, who had the door on her shoulder, began to be very tired; but she thought it was the nuts upon it that were so heavy: so she said softly, "Frederick, I must let the nuts go."
73:73	D:A	D:F	"No," answered he, "not now, they will discover us."
74:74	D:A	D:F	"I can"t help that: they must go."
75:75	A:A	D:F	"Well, then, make haste and throw them down, if you will."
76:76	D:Su+	D:F	Then away rattled the nuts down among the boughs and one of the thieves cried, "Bless me, it is hailing."
77:77	N:Sa	N:F	A little while after, Catherine thought the door was still very heavy: so she whispered to Frederick, "I must throw the vinegar down."
78:78	D:A	N:F	"Pray don"t," answered he, "it will discover us."
79:79	D:Sa	N:F	"I can"t help that," said she, "go it must."
80:80	N:Su-	N:F	So she poured all the vinegar down; and the thieves said, "What a heavy dew there is!"

82:82	A:F	D:F	But he begged and prayed her not to do so, for he was sure it would betray them.
83:83	F:F	F:H	"Here goes, however," said she: and down went the door with such a clatter upon the thieves, that they cried out "Murder!" and not knowing what was coming, ran away as fast as they could, and left all the gold.
84:84	H:H	H:H	So when Frederick and Catherine came down, there they found all their money safe and sound.
0:0	Su-:Su-	N:N	There was once a forester who went into the forest to hunt, and as he entered it he heard a sound of screaming as if a little child were there.
1:1	Su-:N	N:N	He followed the sound, and at last came to a high tree, and at the top of this a little child was sitting, for the mother had fallen asleep under the tree with the child, and a bird of prey had seen it in her arms, had flown down, snatched it away, and set it on the high tree.
2:2	H:H	H:N	The forester climbed up, brought the child down, and thought to himself: "You will take him home with you, and bring him up with your Lina."
3:3	N:N	N:N	He took it home, therefore, and the two children grew up together.
4:4	N:N	N:N	And the one, which he had found on a tree was called Fundevogel, because a bird had carried it away.
5:5	N:Sa	N:N	Fundevogel and Lina loved each other so dearly that when they did not see each other they were sad.
6:6	N:N	N:N	Now the forester had an old cook, who one evening took two pails and began to fetch water, and did not go once only, but many times, out to the spring.
7:7	Su-:Su+	N:N	Lina saw this and said, "Listen, old Sanna, why are you fetching so much water?"

9:9	D:Su-	D:-	So Lina said, no, she would never repeat it to anyone, and then the cook said: "Early tomorrow morning, when the forester is out hunting, I will heat the water, and when it is boiling in the kettle, I will throw in Fundevogel, and will boil him in it."
10:10	N:N	N:F	Early next morning the forester got up and went out hunting, and when he was gone the children were still in bed.
11:11	N:F	N:F	Then Lina said to Fundevogel: "If you will never leave me, I too will never leave you."

13:13	N:F	N:F	Then said Lina: "Then will I tell you.
14:14	F:F	F:F	Last night, old Sanna carried so many buckets of water into the house that I asked her why she was doing that, and she said that if I would promise not to tell anyone, and she said that early tomorrow morning when father was out hunting, she would set the kettle full of water, throw you into it and boil you; but we will get up quickly, dress ourselves, and go away together."
15:15	N:N	F:H	The two children therefore got up, dressed themselves quickly, and went away.
16:16	N:N	F:N	When the water in the kettle was boiling, the cook went into the bedroom to fetch Fundevogel and throw him into it.
17:17	Su-:A	-:H	But when she came in, and went to the beds, both the children were gone.
18:18	F:F	-:H	Then she was terribly alarmed, and she said to herself: "What shall I say now when the forester comes home and sees that the children are gone?
19:19	F:F	F:H	They must be followed instantly to get them back again."
20:20	N:F	N:F	Then the cook sent three servants after them, who were to run and overtake the children.
21:21	N:F	N:F	The children, however, were sitting outside the forest, and when they saw from afar the three servants running, Lina said to Fundevogel: "Never leave me, and I will never leave you."
22:22	H:H	N:F	Fundevogel said: "Neither now, nor ever."
23:23	N:N	N:N	Then said Lina: "Do you become a rose-tree, and I the rose upon it."

25:25	D:Su-	D:+	Then said they: "There is nothing to be done here," and they went home and told the cook that they had seen nothing in the forest but a little rose-bush with one rose on it.
26:26	A:A	A:F	Then the old cook scolded and said: "You simpletons, you should have cut the rose-bush in two, and have broken off the rose and brought it home with you; go, and do it at once."
27:27	N:N	N:F	They had therefore to go out and look for the second time.
28:28	N:N	N:F	The children, however, saw them coming from a distance.

30:30	H:H	N:F	Fundevogel said: "Neither now; nor ever."

32:32	N:Su-	N:+	So when the three servants came, nothing was there but a church, with a chandelier in it.
33:33	D:Su-	N:H	They said therefore to each other: "What can we do here, let us go home."

37:37	N:F	N:F	The children, however, saw from afar that the three servants were coming, and the cook waddling after them.
38:38	H:F	N:F	Then said Lina: "Fundevogel, never leave me, and I will never leave you."

43:43	H:H	H:H	Then the children went home together, and were heartily delighted, and if they have not died, they are living still.
0:0	N:N	N:N	Hard by a great forest dwelt a poor wood-cutter with his wife and his two children.
1:1	N:N	N:N	The boy was called Hansel and the girl Gretel.
2:2	Sa:Sa	S:S	He had little to bite and to break, and once when great dearth fell on the land, he could no longer procure even daily bread.
3:3	Sa:Sa	S:S	Now when he thought over this by night in his bed, and tossed about in his anxiety, he groaned and said to his wife: "What is to become of us?
4:4	Sa:Sa	S:S	How are we to feed our poor children, when we no longer have anything even for ourselves?"
5:5	Su-:N	-:S	"I"ll tell you what, husband," answered the woman, "early tomorrow morning we will take the children out into the forest to where it is the thickest; there we will light a fire for them, and give each of them one more piece of bread, and then we will go to our work and leave them alone.
6:6	Su-:H	-:S	They will not find the way home again, and we shall be rid of them."
7:7	Sa:F	S:S	"No, wife," said the man, "I will not do that; how can I bear to leave my children alone in the forest?--the wild animals would soon come and tear them to pieces."
8:8	A:A	A:S	"O, you fool!" said she, "then we must all four die of hunger, you may as well plane the planks for our coffins," and she left him no peace until he consented.
9:9	Sa:Sa	S:S	"But I feel very sorry for the poor children, all the same," said the man.
10:10	N:N	S:S	The two children had also not been able to sleep for hunger, and had heard what their stepmother had said to their father.
11:11	Sa:Sa	S:S	Gretel wept bitter tears, and said to Hansel: "Now all is over with us."
12:12	N:H	N:S	"Be quiet, Gretel," said Hansel, "do not distress yourself, I will soon find a way to help us."
13:13	N:N	N:S	And when the old folks had fallen asleep, he got up, put on his little coat, opened the door below, and crept outside.
14:14	N:N	N:S	The moon shone brightly, and the white pebbles which lay in front of the house glittered like real silver pennies.

16:16	H:H	N:F	Then he went back and said to Gretel: "Be comforted, dear little sister, and sleep in peace, God will not forsake us," and he lay down again in his bed.
17:17	D:A	N:F	When day dawned, but before the sun had risen, the woman came and awoke the two children, saying: "Get up, you sluggards! we are going into the forest to fetch wood."
18:18	N:A	N:F	She gave each a little piece of bread, and said: "There is something for your dinner, but do not eat it up before then, for you will get nothing else."
19:19	N:N	N:S	Gretel took the bread under her apron, as Hansel had the pebbles in his pocket.
20:20	N:N	N:F	Then they all set out together on the way to the forest.
21:21	N:N	N:F	When they had walked a short time, Hansel stood still and peeped back at the house, and did so again and again.
22:22	D:Su+	N:S	His father said: "Hansel, what are you looking at there and staying behind for?

25:25	D:A	D:S	The wife said: "Fool, that is not your little cat, that is the morning sun which is shining on the chimneys."
26:26	N:N	N:+	Hansel, however, had not been looking back at the cat, but had been constantly throwing one of the white pebble-stones out of his pocket on the road.
27:27	N:N	N:N	When they had reached the middle of the forest, the father said: "Now, children, pile up some wood, and I will light a fire that you may not be cold."

29:29	N:N	N:N	The brushwood was lighted, and when the flames were burning very high, the woman said: "Now, children, lay yourselves down by the fire and rest, we will go into the forest and cut some wood.
30:30	N:N	N:N	When we have done, we will come back and fetch you away."
31:31	N:Su+	N:F	Hansel and Gretel sat by the fire, and when noon came, each ate a little piece of bread, and as they heard the strokes of the wood-axe they believed that their father was near.
32:32	Su-:Su-	N:S	It was not the axe, however, but a branch which he had fastened to a withered tree which the wind was blowing backwards and forwards.
33:33	N:N	N:F	And as they had been sitting such a long time, their eyes closed with fatigue, and they fell fast asleep.

0:0	N:N	N:N	Some men are born to good luck: all they do or try to do comes right-- all that falls to them is so much gain--all their geese are swans--all their cards are trumps--toss them which way you will, they will always, like poor puss, alight upon their legs, and only move on so much the faster.
1:1	N:N	N:N	The world may very likely not always think of them as they think of themselves, but what care they for the world? what can it know about the matter?
2:2	N:N	N:N	One of these lucky beings was neighbour Hans.
3:3	N:N	N:N	Seven long years he had worked hard for his master.
4:4	Sa:Sa	N:S	At last he said, "Master, my time is up; I must go home and see my poor mother once more: so pray pay me my wages and let me go."
5:5	N:H	N:S	And the master said, "You have been a faithful and good servant, Hans, so your pay shall be handsome."
6:6	N:N	N:H	Then he gave him a lump of silver as big as his head.

8:8	N:N	N:H	As he went lazily on, dragging one foot after another, a man came in sight, trotting gaily along on a capital horse.

10:10	N:H	N:H	There he sits as easy and happy as if he was at home, in the chair by his fireside; he trips against no stones, saves shoe-leather, and gets on he hardly knows how."
11:11	Su-:Su+	N:H	Hans did not speak so softly but the horseman heard it all, and said, "Well, friend, why do you go on foot then?"

21:21	Su-:Su-	-:-	Away went the horse full gallop; and before Hans knew what he was about, he was thrown off, and lay on his back by the road-side.
22:22	N:H	-:+	His horse would have ran off, if a shepherd who was coming by, driving a cow, had not stopped it.
23:23	Sa:Sa	N:+	Hans soon came to himself, and got upon his legs again, sadly vexed, and said to the shepherd, "This riding is no joke, when a man has the luck to get upon a beast like this that stumbles and flings him off as if it would break his neck.

32:32	H:H	H:H	"If I have only a piece of bread (and I certainly shall always be able to get that), I can, whenever I like, eat my butter and cheese with it; and when I am thirsty I can milk my cow and drink the milk: and what can I wish for more?"

0:0	N:N	N:N	There was once upon a time a king who had a great forest near his palace, full of all kinds of wild animals.
1:1	F:N	F:N	One day he sent out a huntsman to shoot him a roe, but he did not come back.
2:2	F:N	F:N	"Perhaps some accident has befallen him," said the king, and the next day he sent out two more huntsmen who were to search for him, but they too stayed away.
3:3	F:F	F:F	Then on the third day, he sent for all his huntsmen, and said: "Scour the whole forest through, and do not give up until you have found all three."
4:4	F:N	F:F	But of these also, none came home again, none were seen again.
5:5	N:N	F:N	From that time forth, no one would any longer venture into the forest, and it lay there in deep stillness and solitude, and nothing was seen of it, but sometimes an eagle or a hawk flying over it.
6:6	N:N	F:F	This lasted for many years, when an unknown huntsman announced himself to the king as seeking a situation, and offered to go into the dangerous forest.
7:7	F:F	F:F	The king, however, would not give his consent, and said: "It is not safe in there; I fear it would fare with you no better than with the others, and you would never come out again."
8:8	A:F	F:F	The huntsman replied: "Lord, I will venture it at my own risk, of fear I know nothing."
9:9	N:N	F:F	The huntsman therefore betook himself with his dog to the forest.
10:10	F:N	F:F	It was not long before the dog fell in with some game on the way, and wanted to pursue it; but hardly had the dog run two steps when it stood before a deep pool, could go no farther, and a naked arm stretched itself out of the water, seized it, and drew it under.
11:11	F:N	F:F	When the huntsman saw that, he went back and fetched three men to come with buckets and bale out the water.
12:12	D:N	F:N	When they could see to the bottom there lay a wild man whose body was brown like rusty iron, and whose hair hung over his face down to his knees.
13:13	N:N	F:N	They bound him with cords, and led him away to the castle.
14:14	Su-:N	F:N	There was great astonishment over the wild man; the king, however, had him put in an iron cage in his courtyard, and forbade the door to be opened on pain of death, and the queen herself was to take the key into her keeping.
15:15	H:N	H:N	And from this time forth everyone could again go into the forest with safety.
16:16	Sa:N	H:N	The king had a son of eight years, who was once playing in the courtyard, and while he was playing, his golden ball fell into the cage.
17:17	A:N	F:N	The boy ran thither and said: "Give me my ball out."
18:18	A:N	F:N	"Not till you have opened the door for me," answered the man.
19:19	A:N	F:N	"No," said the boy, "I will not do that; the king has forbidden it," and ran away.
20:20	F:N	F:N	The next day he again went and asked for his ball; the wild man said: "Open my door," but the boy would not.
21:21	Sa:N	F:N	On the third day the king had ridden out hunting, and the boy went once more and said: "I cannot open the door even if I wished, for I have not the key."
22:22	N:N	F:N	Then the wild man said: "It lies under your mother"s pillow, you can get it there."
23:23	Sa:N	F:N	The boy, who wanted to have his ball back, cast all thought to the winds, and brought the key.
24:24	Sa:N	F:N	The door opened with difficulty, and the boy pinched his fingers.
25:25	H:N	F:N	When it was open the wild man stepped out, gave him the golden ball, and hurried away.
26:26	F:F	F:F	The boy had become afraid; he called and cried after him: "Oh, wild man, do not go away, or I shall be beaten!"
27:27	N:N	F:N	The wild man turned back, took him up, set him on his shoulder, and went with hasty steps into the forest.

29:29	Su-:N	A:F	She knew nothing about it, and sought the key, but it was gone.

0:0	N:N	N:S	There was once an old castle, that stood in the middle of a deep gloomy wood, and in the castle lived an old fairy.
1:1	N:N	N:N	Now this fairy could take any shape she pleased.
2:2	N:N	N:N	All the day long she flew about in the form of an owl, or crept about the country like a cat; but at night she always became an old woman again.
3:3	A:N	F:N	When any young man came within a hundred paces of her castle, he became quite fixed, and could not move a step till she came and set him free; which she would not do till he had given her his word never to come there again: but when any pretty maiden came within that space she was changed into a bird, and the fairy put her into a cage, and hung her up in a chamber in the castle.
4:4	N:N	F:N	There were seven hundred of these cages hanging in the castle, and all with beautiful birds in them.
5:5	N:N	F:N	Now there was once a maiden whose name was Jorinda.
6:6	H:N	F:H	She was prettier than all the pretty girls that ever were seen before, and a shepherd lad, whose name was Jorindel, was very fond of her, and they were soon to be married.
7:7	F:F	F:F	One day they went to walk in the wood, that they might be alone; and Jorindel said, "We must take care that we don"t go too near to the fairy"s castle."
8:8	H:N	F:H	It was a beautiful evening; the last rays of the setting sun shone bright through the long stems of the trees upon the green underwood beneath, and the turtle-doves sang from the tall birches.

10:10	Sa:F	F:S	They had wandered a long way; and when they looked to see which way they should go home, they found themselves at a loss to know what path to take.
11:11	Su-:N	F:N	The sun was setting fast, and already half of its circle had sunk behind the hill: Jorindel on a sudden looked behind him, and saw through the bushes that they had, without knowing it, sat down close under the old walls of the castle.

21:21	F:N	F:F	Tu whu!
22:22	F:N	F:N	Tu whu!"
23:23	F:N	F:S	Jorindel could not move; he stood fixed as a stone, and could neither weep, nor speak, nor stir hand or foot.
24:24	A:N	F:N	And now the sun went quite down; the gloomy night came; the owl flew into a bush; and a moment after the old fairy came forth pale and meagre, with staring eyes, and a nose and chin that almost met one another.
25:25	A:N	F:S	She mumbled something to herself, seized the nightingale, and went away with it in her hand.

27:27	F:N	F:S	He could not speak, he could not move from the spot where he stood.

35:35	F:Sa	S:S	"Alas!" he said, "what will become of me?"

39:39	H:H	+:H	In the morning when he awoke, he began to search over hill and dale for this pretty flower; and eight long days he sought for it in vain: but on the ninth day, early in the morning, he found the beautiful purple flower; and in the middle of it was a large dewdrop, as big as a costly pearl.

0:0	N:N	N:N	A merchant, who had three daughters, was once setting out upon a journey; but before he went he asked each daughter what gift he should bring back for her.
1:1	N:N	N:N	The eldest wished for pearls; the second for jewels; but the third, who was called Lily, said, "Dear father, bring me a rose."
2:2	N:N	N:N	Now it was no easy task to find a rose, for it was the middle of winter; yet as she was his prettiest daughter, and was very fond of flowers, her father said he would try what he could do.
3:3	N:N	N:N	So he kissed all three, and bid them goodbye.
4:4	N:Sa	N:S	And when the time came for him to go home, he had bought pearls and jewels for the two eldest, but he had sought everywhere in vain for the rose; and when he went into any garden and asked for such a thing, the people laughed at him, and asked him whether he thought roses grew in snow.
5:5	Sa:Sa	S:S	This grieved him very much, for Lily was his dearest child; and as he was journeying home, thinking what he should bring her, he came to a fine castle; and around the castle was a garden, in one half of which it seemed to be summer-time and in the other half winter.
6:6	N:N	N:N	On one side the finest flowers were in full bloom, and on the other everything looked dreary and buried in the snow.
7:7	H:H	H:H	"A lucky hit!" said he, as he called to his servant, and told him to go to a beautiful bed of roses that was there, and bring him away one of the finest flowers.
8:8	A:F	F:F	This done, they were riding away well pleased, when up sprang a fierce lion, and roared out, "Whoever has stolen my roses shall be eaten up alive!"
9:9	N:F	F:F	Then the man said, "I knew not that the garden belonged to you; can nothing save my life?"

11:11	F:Sa	F:S	But the man was unwilling to do so and said, "It may be my youngest daughter, who loves me most, and always runs to meet me when I go home."

18:18	N:N	F:S	But she comforted him, and said, "Dear father, the word you have given must be kept; I will go to the lion, and soothe him: perhaps he will let me come safe home again."
19:19	H:N	N:S	The next morning she asked the way she was to go, and took leave of her father, and went forth with a bold heart into the wood.
20:20	N:Su+	N:+	But the lion was an enchanted prince.
21:21	N:N	F:N	By day he and all his court were lions, but in the evening they took their right forms again.
22:22	H:H	H:H	And when Lily came to the castle, he welcomed her so courteously that she agreed to marry him.
23:23	H:H	H:H	The wedding-feast was held, and they lived happily together a long time.
24:24	N:N	N:N	The prince was only to be seen as soon as evening came, and then he held his court; but every morning he left his bride, and went away by himself, she knew not whither, till the night came again.

26:26	H:H	H:H	Then she rejoiced much at the thoughts of seeing her father once more, and set out with the lions; and everyone was overjoyed to see her, for they had thought her dead long since.
27:27	H:H	H:H	But she told them how happy she was, and stayed till the feast was over, and then went back to the wood.
28:28	N:N	N:N	Her second sister was soon after married, and when Lily was asked to go to the wedding, she said to the prince, "I will not go alone this time--you must go with me."
29:29	F:F	F:F	But he would not, and said that it would be a very hazardous thing; for if the least ray of the torch-light should fall upon him his enchantment would become still worse, for he should be changed into a dove, and be forced to wander about the world for seven long years.

33:33	N:Sa	N:S	In a moment he disappeared, and when his wife came in and looked for him, she found only a white dove; and it said to her, "Seven years must I fly up and down over the face of the earth, but every now and then I will let fall a white feather, that will show you the way I am going; follow it, and at last you may overtake and set me free."
34:34	Sa:Sa	S:S	This said, he flew out at the door, and poor Lily followed; and every now and then a white feather fell, and showed her the way she was to journey.
35:35	N:Sa	N:S	Thus she went roving on through the wide world, and looked neither to the right hand nor to the left, nor took any rest, for seven years.
36:36	H:H	S:H	Then she began to be glad, and thought to herself that the time was fast coming when all her troubles should end; yet repose was still far off, for one day as she was travelling on she missed the white feather, and when she lifted up her eyes she could nowhere see the dove.
37:37	Sa:Sa	S:S	"Now," thought she to herself, "no aid of man can be of use to me."
38:38	N:N	S:N	So she went to the sun and said, "Thou shinest everywhere, on the hill"s top and the valley"s depth--hast thou anywhere seen my white dove?"

40:40	Sa:N	S:N	So she thanked the sun, and went on her way till eventide; and when the moon arose, she cried unto it, and said, "Thou shinest through the night, over field and grove--hast thou nowhere seen my white dove?"
41:41	N:N	S:N	"No," said the moon, "I cannot help thee but I will give thee an egg-- break it when need comes."
42:42	N:N	S:N	Then she thanked the moon, and went on till the night-wind blew; and she raised up her voice to it, and said, "Thou blowest through every tree and under every leaf--hast thou not seen my white dove?"

45:45	N:N	N:N	Then the night-wind said, "I will give thee counsel.
46:46	N:H	N:H	Go to the Red Sea; on the right shore stand many rods--count them, and when thou comest to the eleventh, break it off, and smite the dragon with it; and so the lion will have the victory, and both of them will appear to you in their own forms.
47:47	N:H	N:H	Then look round and thou wilt see a griffin, winged like bird, sitting by the Red Sea; jump on to his back with thy beloved one as quickly as possible, and he will carry you over the waters to your home.

49:49	N:N	N:N	"When you are half-way over, throw it down, and out of the waters will immediately spring up a high nut-tree on which the griffin will be able to rest, otherwise he would not have the strength to bear you the whole way; if, therefore, thou dost forget to throw down the nut, he will let you both fall into the sea."
50:50	N:N	N:N	So our poor wanderer went forth, and found all as the night-wind had said; and she plucked the eleventh rod, and smote the dragon, and the lion forthwith became a prince, and the dragon a princess again.
