52:52	Sa:Sa	S:S	Thus the unhappy traveller was again forsaken and forlorn; but she took heart and said, "As far as the wind blows, and so long as the cock crows, I will journey on, till I find him once again."
53:53	N:N	S:N	She went on for a long, long way, till at length she came to the castle whither the princess had carried the prince; and there was a feast got ready, and she heard that the wedding was about to be held.
54:54	F:Su+	F:+	"Heaven aid me now!" said she; and she took the casket that the sun had given her, and found that within it lay a dress as dazzling as the sun itself.
55:55	H:H	F:H	So she put it on, and went into the palace, and all the people gazed upon her; and the dress pleased the bride so much that she asked whether it was to be sold.

58:58	N:N	N:N	At last the princess agreed, but she told her chamberlain to give the prince a sleeping draught, that he might not hear or see her.
59:59	N:N	N:N	When evening came, and the prince had fallen asleep, she was led into his chamber, and she sat herself down at his feet, and said: "I have followed thee seven years.
60:60	N:N	N:N	I have been to the sun, the moon, and the night-wind, to seek thee, and at last I have helped thee to overcome the dragon.
61:61	N:Sa	D:S	Wilt thou then forget me quite?"

63:63	Sa:Sa	S:S	Then poor Lily was led away, and forced to give up the golden dress; and when she saw that there was no help for her, she went out into a meadow, and sat herself down and wept.

0:0	H:H	H:H	Once upon a time there was a dear little girl who was loved by everyone who looked at her, but most of all by her grandmother, and there was nothing that she would not have given to the child.

3:3	N:N	N:N	Set out before it gets hot, and when you are going, walk nicely and quietly and do not run off the path, or you may fall and break the bottle, and then your grandmother will get nothing; and when you go into her room, don"t forget to say, "Good morning", and don"t peep into every corner before you do it."

0:0	N:N	N:N	Once upon a time there was a widow who had two daughters; one of them was beautiful and industrious, the other ugly and lazy.
1:1	N:H	N:H	The mother, however, loved the ugly and lazy one best, because she was her own daughter, and so the other, who was only her stepdaughter, was made to do all the work of the house, and was quite the Cinderella of the family.
2:2	N:N	N:S	Her stepmother sent her out every day to sit by the well in the high road, there to spin until she made her fingers bleed.
3:3	N:N	N:S	Now it chanced one day that some blood fell on to the spindle, and as the girl stopped over the well to wash it off, the spindle suddenly sprang out of her hand and fell into the well.

0:0	N:N	N:N	A shepherd had a faithful dog, called Sultan, who was grown very old, and had lost all his teeth.

4:4	F:F	F:S	Poor Sultan, who was lying close by them, heard all that the shepherd and his wife said to one another, and was very much frightened to think tomorrow would be his last day; so in the evening he went to his good friend the wolf, who lived in the wood, and told him all his sorrows, and how his master meant to kill him in the morning.

0:0	N:N	N:N	It was the middle of winter, when the broad flakes of snow were falling around, that the queen of a country many thousand miles off sat working at her window.

13:13	A:A	A:A	When she heard this she turned pale with rage and envy, and called to one of her servants, and said, "Take Snowdrop away into the wide wood, that I may never see her any more."
14:14	Sa:N	S:H	Then the servant led her away; but his heart melted when Snowdrop begged him to spare her life, and he said, "I will not hurt you, thou pretty child."
15:15	F:N	S:N	So he left her by herself; and though he thought it most likely that the wild beasts would tear her in pieces, he felt as if a great weight were taken off his heart when he had made up his mind not to kill her but to leave her to her fate, with the chance of someone finding and saving her.
16:16	F:F	S:F	Then poor Snowdrop wandered along through the wood in great fear; and the wild beasts roared about her, but none did her any harm.
17:17	F:N	F:N	In the evening she came to a cottage among the hills, and went in to rest, for her little feet would carry her no further.
18:18	N:N	N:N	Everything was spruce and neat in the cottage: on the table was spread a white cloth, and there were seven little plates, seven little loaves, and seven little glasses with wine in them; and seven knives and forks laid in order; and by the wall stood seven little beds.
19:19	N:N	N:N	As she was very hungry, she picked a little piece of each loaf and drank a very little wine out of each glass; and after that she thought she would lie down and rest.
20:20	H:N	H:N	So she tried all the little beds; but one was too long, and another was too short, till at last the seventh suited her: and there she laid herself down and went to sleep.
21:21	F:N	F:N	By and by in came the masters of the cottage.
22:22	N:N	F:N	Now they were seven little dwarfs, that lived among the mountains, and dug and searched for gold.
23:23	F:N	F:-	They lighted up their seven lamps, and saw at once that all was not right.
24:24	A:A	A:A	The first said, "Who has been sitting on my stool?"
25:25	A:A	A:A	The second, "Who has been eating off my plate?"
26:26	A:A	A:A	The third, "Who has been picking my bread?"
27:27	A:A	A:A	The fourth, "Who has been meddling with my spoon?"
28:28	A:A	A:A	The fifth, "Who has been handling my fork?"
29:29	A:A	A:A	The sixth, "Who has been cutting with my knife?"
30:30	A:A	A:A	The seventh, "Who has been drinking my wine?"

32:32	A:A	A:A	And the rest came running to him, and everyone cried out that somebody had been upon his bed.

34:34	H:H	H:H	And they were very glad to see her, and took care not to wake her; and the seventh dwarf slept an hour with each of the other dwarfs in turn, till the night was gone.
35:35	Sa:Sa	H:H	In the morning Snowdrop told them all her story; and they pitied her, and said if she would keep all things in order, and cook and wash and knit and spin for them, she might stay where she was, and they would take good care of her.
36:36	F:N	H:N	Then they went out all day long to their work, seeking for gold and silver in the mountains: but Snowdrop was left at home; and they warned her, and said, "The queen will soon find out where you are, so take care and let no one in."
37:37	F:N	F:N	But the queen, now that she thought Snowdrop was dead, believed that she must be the handsomest lady in the land; and she went to her glass and said:
38:38	N:N	F:N	"Tell me, glass, tell me true!
39:39	N:N	F:N	Of all the ladies in the land, Who is fairest, tell me, who?"
40:40	N:N	F:N	And the glass answered:

42:42	F:F	F:F	Then the queen was very much frightened; for she knew that the glass always spoke the truth, and was sure that the servant had betrayed her.
43:43	A:N	F:A	And she could not bear to think that anyone lived who was more beautiful than she was; so she dressed herself up as an old pedlar, and went her way over the hills, to the place where the dwarfs dwelt.

57:57	A:A	A:A	Then the blood ran cold in her heart with spite and malice, to see that Snowdrop still lived; and she dressed herself up again, but in quite another dress from the one she wore before, and took with her a poisoned comb.

67:67	F:F	F:F	Then she dressed herself up as a peasant"s wife, and travelled over the hills to the dwarfs" cottage, and knocked at the door; but Snowdrop put her head out of the window and said, "I dare not let anyone in, for the dwarfs have told me not."

73:73	F:N	F:N	Now the apple was so made up that one side was good, though the other side was poisoned.

75:75	Sa:N	S:F	But she had scarcely put the piece into her mouth, when she fell down dead upon the ground.
76:76	H:N	S:F	"This time nothing will save thee," said the queen; and she went home to her glass, and at last it said:

79:79	F:F	S:F	When evening came, and the dwarfs had gone home, they found Snowdrop lying on the ground: no breath came from her lips, and they were afraid that she was quite dead.
80:80	Sa:N	S:S	They lifted her up, and combed her hair, and washed her face with wine and water; but all was in vain, for the little girl seemed quite dead.
81:81	Sa:Sa	S:S	So they laid her down upon a bier, and all seven watched and bewailed her three whole days; and then they thought they would bury her: but her cheeks were still rosy; and her face looked just as it did while she was alive; so they said, "We will never bury her in the cold ground."
82:82	Sa:N	S:S	And they made a coffin of glass, so that they might still look at her, and wrote upon it in golden letters what her name was, and that she was a king"s daughter.
83:83	Sa:N	S:S	And the coffin was set among the hills, and one of the dwarfs always sat by it and watched.
84:84	Sa:Sa	S:S	And the birds of the air came too, and bemoaned Snowdrop; and first of all came an owl, and then a raven, and at last a dove, and sat by her side.
85:85	N:N	S:S	And thus Snowdrop lay for a long, long time, and still only looked as though she was asleep; for she was even now as white as snow, and as red as blood, and as black as ebony.
86:86	Su+:N	S:N	At last a prince came and called at the dwarfs" house; and he saw Snowdrop, and read what was written in golden letters.

89:89	H:N	H:H	And the prince said, "Thou art quite safe with me."

91:91	H:N	H:H	And Snowdrop consented, and went home with the prince; and everything was got ready with great pomp and splendour for their wedding.
92:92	N:N	H:N	To the feast was asked, among the rest, Snowdrop"s old enemy the queen; and as she was dressing herself in fine rich clothes, she looked in the glass and said:
93:93	N:N	H:N	"Tell me, glass, tell me true!

97:97	A:A	F:A	When she heard this she started with rage; but her envy and curiosity were so great, that she could not help setting out to see the bride.
98:98	H:A	H:H	And when she got there, and saw that it was no other than Snowdrop, who, as she thought, had been dead a long while, she choked with rage, and fell down and died: but Snowdrop and the prince lived and reigned happily over that land many, many years; and sometimes they went up into the mountains, and paid a visit to the little dwarfs, who had been so kind to Snowdrop in her time of need.
0:0	N:N	N:N	There was once upon a time a woman who was a real witch and had two daughters, one ugly and wicked, and this one she loved because she was her own daughter, and one beautiful and good, and this one she hated, because she was her stepdaughter.
1:1	A:A	N:A	The stepdaughter once had a pretty apron, which the other fancied so much that she became envious, and told her mother that she must and would have that apron.
2:2	A:N	N:A	"Be quiet, my child," said the old woman, "and you shall have it.
3:3	A:N	F:-	Your stepsister has long deserved death; tonight when she is asleep I will come and cut her head off.
4:4	F:N	F:-	Only be careful that you are at the far side of the bed, and push her well to the front."
5:5	F:N	F:+	It would have been all over with the poor girl if she had not just then been standing in a corner, and heard everything.
6:6	F:N	F:+	All day long she dared not go out of doors, and when bedtime had come, the witch"s daughter got into bed first, so as to lie at the far side, but when she was asleep, the other pushed her gently to the front, and took for herself the place at the back, close by the wall.
7:7	F:N	F:-	In the night, the old woman came creeping in, she held an axe in her right hand, and felt with her left to see if anyone were lying at the outside, and then she grasped the axe with both hands, and cut her own child"s head off.
8:8	F:N	F:-	When she had gone away, the girl got up and went to her sweetheart, who was called Roland, and knocked at his door.
9:9	F:F	F:-	When he came out, she said to him: "Listen, dearest Roland, we must fly in all haste; my stepmother wanted to kill me, but has struck her own child.
10:10	F:N	F:F	When daylight comes, and she sees what she has done, we shall be lost."
11:11	F:F	F:F	"But," said Roland, "I counsel you first to take away her magic wand, or we cannot escape if she pursues us."
12:12	F:N	F:-	The maiden fetched the magic wand, and she took the dead girl"s head and dropped three drops of blood on the ground, one in front of the bed, one in the kitchen, and one on the stairs.
13:13	F:N	F:F	Then she hurried away with her lover.
14:14	N:N	F:S	When the old witch got up next morning, she called her daughter, and wanted to give her the apron, but she did not come.
15:15	A:Sa	F:S	Then the witch cried: "Where are you?"

17:17	A:Sa	F:S	The old woman went out, but saw no one on the stairs, and cried again: "Where are you?"
18:18	N:N	F:N	"Here in the kitchen, I am warming myself," cried the second drop of blood.

20:20	F:F	F:S	Then she cried again: "Where are you?"

24:24	D:N	F:S	Her own child, whose head she had cut off, bathed in her blood.
25:25	A:A	F:A	The witch fell into a passion, sprang to the window, and as she could look forth quite far into the world, she perceived her stepdaughter hurrying away with her sweetheart Roland.
26:26	A:A	A:A	"That shall not help you," cried she, "even if you have got a long way off, you shall still not escape me."

29:29	A:N	F:+	The witch placed herself on the shore, threw breadcrumbs in, and went to endless trouble to entice the duck; but the duck did not let herself be enticed, and the old woman had to go home at night as she had come.
30:30	F:N	F:N	At this the girl and her sweetheart Roland resumed their natural shapes again, and they walked on the whole night until daybreak.
31:31	N:N	F:N	Then the maiden changed herself into a beautiful flower which stood in the midst of a briar hedge, and her sweetheart Roland into a fiddler.
32:32	A:N	F:N	It was not long before the witch came striding up towards them, and said to the musician: "Dear musician, may I pluck that beautiful flower for myself?"

34:34	A:N	A:N	As she was hastily creeping into the hedge and was just going to pluck the flower, knowing perfectly well who the flower was, he began to play, and whether she would or not, she was forced to dance, for it was a magical dance.
35:35	A:N	A:-	The faster he played, the more violent springs was she forced to make, and the thorns tore her clothes from her body, and pricked her and wounded her till she bled, and as he did not stop, she had to dance till she lay dead on the ground.

38:38	N:N	H:H	Then Roland went away, and the girl stood like a red landmark in the field and waited for her beloved.
39:39	H:N	S:S	But when Roland got home, he fell into the snares of another, who so fascinated him that he forgot the maiden.
40:40	Sa:Sa	S:S	The poor girl remained there a long time, but at length, as he did not return at all, she was sad, and changed herself into a flower, and thought: "Someone will surely come this way, and trample me down."
41:41	N:N	S:N	It befell, however, that a shepherd kept his sheep in the field and saw the flower, and as it was so pretty, plucked it, took it with him, and laid it away in his chest.
42:42	F:N	F:N	From that time forth, strange things happened in the shepherd"s house.
43:43	Su+:N	+:N	When he arose in the morning, all the work was already done, the room was swept, the table and benches cleaned, the fire in the hearth was lighted, and the water was fetched, and at noon, when he came home, the table was laid, and a good dinner served.
44:44	Su+:Su+	+:+	He could not conceive how this came to pass, for he never saw a human being in his house, and no one could have concealed himself in it.
45:45	F:F	+:F	He was certainly pleased with this good attendance, but still at last he was so afraid that he went to a wise woman and asked for her advice.
46:46	N:N	F:N	The wise woman said: "There is some enchantment behind it, listen very early some morning if anything is moving in the room, and if you see anything, no matter what it is, throw a white cloth over it, and then the magic will be stopped."
47:47	F:N	F:+	The shepherd did as she bade him, and next morning just as day dawned, he saw the chest open, and the flower come out.
48:48	F:N	F:N	Swiftly he sprang towards it, and threw a white cloth over it.
49:49	Su+:N	+:N	Instantly the transformation came to an end, and a beautiful girl stood before him, who admitted to him that she had been the flower, and that up to this time she had attended to his house-keeping.
50:50	Sa:Su-	S:-	She told him her story, and as she pleased him he asked her if she would marry him, but she answered: "No," for she wanted to remain faithful to her sweetheart Roland, although he had deserted her.

57:57	H:H	H:H	Then the faithful maiden held her wedding with her sweetheart Roland, and grief came to an end and joy began.
0:0	N:N	N:N	1.
1:1	N:N	N:N	HOW THEY WENT TO THE MOUNTAINS TO EAT NUTS
2:2	N:N	N:N	"The nuts are quite ripe now," said Chanticleer to his wife Partlet, "suppose we go together to the mountains, and eat as many as we can, before the squirrel takes them all away."
3:3	H:H	H:H	"With all my heart," said Partlet, "let us go and make a holiday of it together."
4:4	N:N	H:H	So they went to the mountains; and as it was a lovely day, they stayed there till the evening.
5:5	N:N	N:H	Now, whether it was that they had eaten so many nuts that they could not walk, or whether they were lazy and would not, I do not know: however, they took it into their heads that it did not become them to go home on foot.
6:6	N:N	N:H	So Chanticleer began to build a little carriage of nutshells: and when it was finished, Partlet jumped into it and sat down, and bid Chanticleer harness himself to it and draw her home.
7:7	Su-:N	-:A	"That"s a good joke!" said Chanticleer; "no, that will never do; I had rather by half walk home; I"ll sit on the box and be coachman, if you like, but I"ll not draw."
8:8	A:A	A:A	While this was passing, a duck came quacking up and cried out, "You thieving vagabonds, what business have you in my grounds?
9:9	A:A	A:A	I"ll give it you well for your insolence!" and upon that she fell upon Chanticleer most lustily.
10:10	F:A	F:A	But Chanticleer was no coward, and returned the duck"s blows with his sharp spurs so fiercely that she soon began to cry out for mercy; which was only granted her upon condition that she would draw the carriage home for them.
11:11	N:N	N:N	This she agreed to do; and Chanticleer got upon the box, and drove, crying, "Now, duck, get on as fast as you can."
12:12	N:N	N:N	And away they went at a pretty good pace.
13:13	Su+:Sa	+:S	After they had travelled along a little way, they met a needle and a pin walking together along the road: and the needle cried out, "Stop, stop!" and said it was so dark that they could hardly find their way, and such dirty walking they could not get on at all: he told them that he and his friend, the pin, had been at a public-house a few miles off, and had sat drinking till they had forgotten how late it was; he begged therefore that the travellers would be so kind as to give them a lift in their carriage.
14:14	N:N	N:N	Chanticleer observing that they were but thin fellows, and not likely to take up much room, told them they might ride, but made them promise not to dirty the wheels of the carriage in getting in, nor to tread on Partlet"s toes.
15:15	F:H	F:H	Late at night they arrived at an inn; and as it was bad travelling in the dark, and the duck seemed much tired, and waddled about a good deal from one side to the other, they made up their minds to fix their quarters there: but the landlord at first was unwilling, and said his house was full, thinking they might not be very respectable company: however, they spoke civilly to him, and gave him the egg which Partlet had laid by the way, and said they would give him the duck, who was in the habit of laying one every day: so at last he let them come in, and they bespoke a handsome supper, and spent the evening very jollily.
16:16	N:N	N:-	Early in the morning, before it was quite light, and when nobody was stirring in the inn, Chanticleer awakened his wife, and, fetching the egg, they pecked a hole in it, ate it up, and threw the shells into the fireplace: they then went to the pin and needle, who were fast asleep, and seizing them by the heads, stuck one into the landlord"s easy chair and the other into his handkerchief; and, having done this, they crept away as softly as possible.
17:17	N:N	N:F	However, the duck, who slept in the open air in the yard, heard them coming, and jumping into the brook which ran close by the inn, soon swam out of their reach.
18:18	N:N	N:S	An hour or two afterwards the landlord got up, and took his handkerchief to wipe his face, but the pin ran into him and pricked him: then he walked into the kitchen to light his pipe at the fire, but when he stirred it up the eggshells flew into his eyes, and almost blinded him.
19:19	A:Sa	A:S	"Bless me!" said he, "all the world seems to have a design against my head this morning": and so saying, he threw himself sulkily into his easy chair; but, oh dear! the needle ran into him; and this time the pain was not in his head.
20:20	A:A	A:A	He now flew into a very great passion, and, suspecting the company who had come in the night before, he went to look after them, but they were all off; so he swore that he never again would take in such a troop of vagabonds, who ate a great deal, paid no reckoning, and gave him nothing for his trouble but their apish tricks.
21:21	N:N	N:N	2.
22:22	N:N	N:N	HOW CHANTICLEER AND PARTLET WENT TO VIST MR KORBES
23:23	N:N	N:N	Another day, Chanticleer and Partlet wished to ride out together; so Chanticleer built a handsome carriage with four red wheels, and harnessed six mice to it; and then he and Partlet got into the carriage, and away they drove.
24:24	N:N	N:N	Soon afterwards a cat met them, and said, "Where are you going?"
25:25	N:N	N:N	And Chanticleer replied,
26:26	N:N	N:N	"All on our way A visit to pay To Mr Korbes, the fox, today."
27:27	N:N	N:H	Then the cat said, "Take me with you," Chanticleer said, "With all my heart: get up behind, and be sure you do not fall off."
28:28	F:N	F:H	"Take care of this handsome coach of mine, Nor dirty my pretty red wheels so fine!
29:29	N:N	N:H	Now, mice, be ready, And, wheels, run steady!
30:30	N:N	N:H	For we are going a visit to pay To Mr Korbes, the fox, today."
31:31	N:N	N:N	Soon after came up a millstone, an egg, a duck, and a pin; and Chanticleer gave them all leave to get into the carriage and go with them.
32:32	N:N	N:N	When they arrived at Mr Korbes"s house, he was not at home; so the mice drew the carriage into the coach-house, Chanticleer and Partlet flew upon a beam, the cat sat down in the fireplace, the duck got into the washing cistern, the pin stuck himself into the bed pillow, the millstone laid himself over the house door, and the egg rolled himself up in the towel.
33:33	N:N	N:-	When Mr Korbes came home, he went to the fireplace to make a fire; but the cat threw all the ashes in his eyes: so he ran to the kitchen to wash himself; but there the duck splashed all the water in his face; and when he tried to wipe himself, the egg broke to pieces in the towel all over his face and eyes.
34:34	A:A	A:A	Then he was very angry, and went without his supper to bed; but when he laid his head on the pillow, the pin ran into his cheek: at this he became quite furious, and, jumping up, would have run out of the house; but when he came to the door, the millstone fell down on his head, and killed him on the spot.
35:35	N:N	N:N	3.
36:36	N:N	N:N	HOW PARTLET DIED AND WAS BURIED, AND HOW CHANTICLEER DIED OF GRIEF
37:37	N:N	N:N	Another day Chanticleer and Partlet agreed to go again to the mountains to eat nuts; and it was settled that all the nuts which they found should be shared equally between them.
38:38	N:N	F:F	Now Partlet found a very large nut; but she said nothing about it to Chanticleer, and kept it all to herself: however, it was so big that she could not swallow it, and it stuck in her throat.
39:39	F:F	F:F	Then she was in a great fright, and cried out to Chanticleer, "Pray run as fast as you can, and fetch me some water, or I shall be choked."
40:40	F:N	F:F	Chanticleer ran as fast as he could to the river, and said, "River, give me some water, for Partlet lies in the mountain, and will be choked by a great nut."
41:41	N:N	F:F	The river said, "Run first to the bride, and ask her for a silken cord to draw up the water."
42:42	N:N	F:F	Chanticleer ran to the bride, and said, "Bride, you must give me a silken cord, for then the river will give me water, and the water I will carry to Partlet, who lies on the mountain, and will be choked by a great nut."
43:43	N:N	N:N	But the bride said, "Run first, and bring me my garland that is hanging on a willow in the garden."
44:44	N:N	S:S	Then Chanticleer ran to the garden, and took the garland from the bough where it hung, and brought it to the bride; and then the bride gave him the silken cord, and he took the silken cord to the river, and the river gave him water, and he carried the water to Partlet; but in the meantime she was choked by the great nut, and lay quite dead, and never moved any more.
45:45	Sa:Sa	S:S	Then Chanticleer was very sorry, and cried bitterly; and all the beasts came and wept with him over poor Partlet.
46:46	N:N	S:S	And six mice built a little hearse to carry her to her grave; and when it was ready they harnessed themselves before it, and Chanticleer drove them.
47:47	N:N	N:S	On the way they met the fox.
48:48	N:N	N:S	"Where are you going, Chanticleer?" said he.
49:49	N:N	N:S	"To bury my Partlet," said the other.
50:50	N:N	N:S	"May I go with you?" said the fox.
51:51	N:N	N:S	"Yes; but you must get up behind, or my horses will not be able to draw you."
52:52	N:N	N:S	Then the fox got up behind; and presently the wolf, the bear, the goat, and all the beasts of the wood, came and climbed upon the hearse.
53:53	N:N	N:S	So on they went till they came to a rapid stream.
54:54	N:N	N:N	"How shall we get over?" said Chanticleer.
55:55	N:N	N:N	Then said a straw, "I will lay myself across, and you may pass over upon me."
56:56	N:N	S:S	But as the mice were going over, the straw slipped away and fell into the water, and the six mice all fell in and were drowned.
57:57	N:N	N:S	What was to be done?
58:58	N:N	N:S	Then a large log of wood came and said, "I am big enough; I will lay myself across the stream, and you shall pass over upon me."
59:59	N:N	N:S	So he laid himself down; but they managed so clumsily, that the log of wood fell in and was carried away by the stream.
60:60	Sa:N	S:S	Then a stone, who saw what had happened, came up and kindly offered to help poor Chanticleer by laying himself across the stream; and this time he got safely to the other side with the hearse, and managed to get Partlet out of it; but the fox and the other mourners, who were sitting behind, were too heavy, and fell back into the water and were all carried away by the stream and drowned.
61:61	N:N	S:S	Thus Chanticleer was left alone with his dead Partlet; and having dug a grave for her, he laid her in it, and made a little hillock over her.
62:62	Sa:Sa	S:S	Then he sat down by the grave, and wept and mourned, till at last he died too; and so all were dead.
0:0	Sa:N	S:N	There was once upon a time a soldier who for many years had served the king faithfully, but when the war came to an end could serve no longer because of the many wounds which he had received.
1:1	N:N	S:N	The king said to him: "You may return to your home, I need you no longer, and you will not receive any more money, for he only receives wages who renders me service for them."
2:2	Sa:F	S:F	Then the soldier did not know how to earn a living, went away greatly troubled, and walked the whole day, until in the evening he entered a forest.
3:3	N:N	S:N	When darkness came on, he saw a light, which he went up to, and came to a house wherein lived a witch.
4:4	F:N	F:N	"Do give me one night"s lodging, and a little to eat and drink," said he to her, "or I shall starve."
5:5	A:Su-	F:N	"Oho!" she answered, "who gives anything to a run-away soldier?
6:6	N:N	F:N	Yet will I be compassionate, and take you in, if you will do what I wish."
7:7	F:N	F:N	"What do you wish?" said the soldier.
8:8	N:N	F:N	"That you should dig all round my garden for me, tomorrow."
9:9	F:N	F:N	The soldier consented, and next day laboured with all his strength, but could not finish it by the evening.
10:10	N:N	F:N	"I see well enough," said the witch, "that you can do no more today, but I will keep you yet another night, in payment for which you must tomorrow chop me a load of wood, and chop it small."
11:11	N:N	F:N	The soldier spent the whole day in doing it, and in the evening the witch proposed that he should stay one night more.
12:12	N:N	F:N	"Tomorrow, you shall only do me a very trifling piece of work.
13:13	N:N	N:N	Behind my house, there is an old dry well, into which my light has fallen, it burns blue, and never goes out, and you shall bring it up again."
14:14	N:N	N:N	Next day the old woman took him to the well, and let him down in a basket.
15:15	H:N	N:N	He found the blue light, and made her a signal to draw him up again.
16:16	F:N	F:N	She did draw him up, but when he came near the edge, she stretched down her hand and wanted to take the blue light away from him.
17:17	F:N	F:N	"No," said he, perceiving her evil intention, "I will not give you the light until I am standing with both feet upon the ground."
18:18	A:A	F:-	The witch fell into a passion, let him fall again into the well, and went away.
19:19	F:N	F:S	The poor soldier fell without injury on the moist ground, and the blue light went on burning, but of what use was that to him?
20:20	F:N	F:S	He saw very well that he could not escape death.
21:21	Sa:Sa	S:S	He sat for a while very sorrowfully, then suddenly he felt in his pocket and found his tobacco pipe, which was still half full.
22:22	Sa:H	S:S	"This shall be my last pleasure," thought he, pulled it out, lit it at the blue light and began to smoke.
23:23	Su+:N	+:+	When the smoke had circled about the cavern, suddenly a little black dwarf stood before him, and said: "Lord, what are your commands?"
24:24	Su+:Su+	+:+	"What my commands are?" replied the soldier, quite astonished.
25:25	N:N	+:N	"I must do everything you bid me," said the little man.
26:26	H:N	+:H	"Good," said the soldier; "then in the first place help me out of this well."
27:27	F:N	F:N	The little man took him by the hand, and led him through an underground passage, but he did not forget to take the blue light with him.
28:28	H:N	H:H	On the way the dwarf showed him the treasures which the witch had collected and hidden there, and the soldier took as much gold as he could carry.
29:29	H:N	H:A	When he was above, he said to the little man: "Now go and bind the old witch, and carry her before the judge."
30:30	F:F	F:H	In a short time she came by like the wind, riding on a wild tom-cat and screaming frightfully.
31:31	N:N	H:N	Nor was it long before the little man reappeared.
32:32	H:N	H:N	"It is all done," said he, "and the witch is already hanging on the gallows.
33:33	N:N	H:N	What further commands has my lord?" inquired the dwarf.
34:34	H:N	H:N	"At this moment, none," answered the soldier; "you can return home, only be at hand immediately, if I summon you."
35:35	N:N	H:N	"Nothing more is needed than that you should light your pipe at the blue light, and I will appear before you at once."
36:36	Su+:N	H:N	Thereupon he vanished from his sight.
37:37	H:N	H:N	The soldier returned to the town from which he come.
38:38	H:N	H:N	He went to the best inn, ordered himself handsome clothes, and then bade the landlord furnish him a room as handsome as possible.
39:39	A:A	A:A	When it was ready and the soldier had taken possession of it, he summoned the little black manikin and said: "I have served the king faithfully, but he has dismissed me, and left me to hunger, and now I want to take my revenge."
40:40	F:N	A:N	"What am I to do?" asked the little man.
41:41	A:N	A:N	"Late at night, when the king"s daughter is in bed, bring her here in her sleep, she shall do servant"s work for me."

43:43	N:N	F:N	When twelve o"clock had struck, the door sprang open, and the manikin carried in the princess.
44:44	A:A	A:A	"Aha! are you there?" cried the soldier, "get to your work at once!
45:45	A:N	A:N	Fetch the broom and sweep the chamber."
46:46	A:N	A:A	When she had done this, he ordered her to come to his chair, and then he stretched out his feet and said: "Pull off my boots," and then he threw them in her face, and made her pick them up again, and clean and brighten them.
47:47	Sa:N	S:A	She, however, did everything he bade her, without opposition, silently and with halfshut eyes.
48:48	N:N	S:N	When the first cock crowed, the manikin carried her back to the royal palace, and laid her in her bed.
49:49	F:N	S:N	Next morning when the princess arose she went to her father, and told him that she had had a very strange dream.
50:50	F:N	F:N	"I was carried through the streets with the rapidity of lightning," said she, "and taken into a soldier"s room, and I had to wait upon him like a servant, sweep his room, clean his boots, and do all kinds of menial work.
51:51	F:N	F:N	It was only a dream, and yet I am just as tired as if I really had done everything."
52:52	N:N	F:N	"The dream may have been true," said the king.
53:53	N:N	F:N	"I will give you a piece of advice.
54:54	N:N	F:N	Fill your pocket full of peas, and make a small hole in the pocket, and then if you are carried away again, they will fall out and leave a track in the streets."
55:55	N:N	F:N	But unseen by the king, the manikin was standing beside him when he said that, and heard all.
56:56	N:N	F:N	At night when the sleeping princess was again carried through the streets, some peas certainly did fall out of her pocket, but they made no track, for the crafty manikin had just before scattered peas in every street there was.
57:57	F:N	F:N	And again the princess was compelled to do servant"s work until cock-crow.
58:58	F:N	F:+	Next morning the king sent his people out to seek the track, but it was all in vain, for in every street poor children were sitting, picking up peas, and saying: "It must have rained peas, last night."
59:59	F:N	F:N	"We must think of something else," said the king; "keep your shoes on when you go to bed, and before you come back from the place where you are taken, hide one of them there, I will soon contrive to find it."
60:60	F:N	F:F	The black manikin heard this plot, and at night when the soldier again ordered him to bring the princess, revealed it to him, and told him that he knew of no expedient to counteract this stratagem, and that if the shoe were found in the soldier"s house it would go badly with him.
61:61	A:N	F:F	"Do what I bid you," replied the soldier, and again this third night the princess was obliged to work like a servant, but before she went away, she hid her shoe under the bed.
62:62	F:N	F:F	Next morning the king had the entire town searched for his daughter"s shoe.
63:63	F:N	F:N	It was found at the soldier"s, and the soldier himself, who at the entreaty of the dwarf had gone outside the gate, was soon brought back, and thrown into prison.
64:64	F:N	F:N	In his flight he had forgotten the most valuable things he had, the blue light and the gold, and had only one ducat in his pocket.
65:65	Su+:N	F:N	And now loaded with chains, he was standing at the window of his dungeon, when he chanced to see one of his comrades passing by.
66:66	Su+:N	F:N	The soldier tapped at the pane of glass, and when this man came up, said to him: "Be so kind as to fetch me the small bundle I have left lying in the inn, and I will give you a ducat for doing it."
67:67	H:N	F:N	His comrade ran thither and brought him what he wanted.
68:68	H:N	F:N	As soon as the soldier was alone again, he lighted his pipe and summoned the black manikin.
69:69	H:N	H:N	"Have no fear," said the latter to his master.

71:71	F:N	F:S	Next day the soldier was tried, and though he had done nothing wicked, the judge condemned him to death.
72:72	F:Sa	F:S	When he was led forth to die, he begged a last favour of the king.
73:73	A:N	F:S	"What is it?" asked the king.

0:0	Sa:N	S:S	A shepherd"s dog had a master who took no care of him, but often let him suffer the greatest hunger.
1:1	Sa:Sa	S:S	At last he could bear it no longer; so he took to his heels, and off he ran in a very sad and sorrowful mood.
2:2	N:Sa	S:S	On the road he met a sparrow that said to him, "Why are you so sad, my friend?"
3:3	Sa:Sa	S:S	"Because," said the dog, "I am very very hungry, and have nothing to eat."
4:4	N:N	N:S	"If that be all," answered the sparrow, "come with me into the next town, and I will soon find you plenty of food."
5:5	N:N	N:N	So on they went together into the town: and as they passed by a butcher"s shop, the sparrow said to the dog, "Stand there a little while till I peck you down a piece of meat."
6:6	N:N	N:N	So the sparrow perched upon the shelf: and having first looked carefully about her to see if anyone was watching her, she pecked and scratched at a steak that lay upon the edge of the shelf, till at last down it fell.
7:7	H:N	N:N	Then the dog snapped it up, and scrambled away with it into a corner, where he soon ate it all up.

11:11	N:H	H:H	"Come with me then," said the sparrow, "and you shall soon have that too."

19:19	F:Su-	F:-	The sparrow, seeing that the carter did not turn out of the way, but would go on in the track in which the dog lay, so as to drive over him, called out, "Stop! stop!

0:0	Sa:N	S:N	There was once a shoemaker, who worked very hard and was very honest: but still he could not earn enough to live upon; and at last all he had in the world was gone, save just leather enough to make one pair of shoes.
1:1	N:N	N:N	Then he cut his leather out, all ready to make up the next day, meaning to rise early in the morning to his work.
2:2	H:H	H:H	His conscience was clear and his heart light amidst all his troubles; so he went peaceably to bed, left all his cares to Heaven, and soon fell asleep.
3:3	Su+:Su+	+:+	In the morning after he had said his prayers, he sat himself down to his work; when, to his great wonder, there stood the shoes all ready made, upon the table.
4:4	Su+:Su+	+:+	The good man knew not what to say or think at such an odd thing happening.
5:5	H:Su+	H:+	He looked at the workmanship; there was not one false stitch in the whole job; all was so neat and true, that it was quite a masterpiece.
6:6	N:H	N:H	The same day a customer came in, and the shoes suited him so well that he willingly paid a price higher than usual for them; and the poor shoemaker, with the money, bought leather enough to make two pairs more.
7:7	N:N	N:N	In the evening he cut out the work, and went to bed early, that he might get up and begin betimes next day; but he was saved all the trouble, for when he got up in the morning the work was done ready to his hand.
8:8	N:N	N:N	Soon in came buyers, who paid him handsomely for his goods, so that he bought leather enough for four pair more.
9:9	N:N	N:N	He cut out the work again overnight and found it done in the morning, as before; and so it went on for some time: what was got ready in the evening was always done by daybreak, and the good man soon became thriving and well off again.

0:0	N:D	S:D	A farmer had a horse that had been an excellent faithful servant to him: but he was now grown too old to work; so the farmer would give him nothing more to eat, and said, "I want you no longer, so take yourself off out of my stable; I shall not take you back again until you are stronger than a lion."
1:1	N:D	S:D	Then he opened the door and turned him adrift.
2:2	Sa:Sa	S:S	The poor horse was very melancholy, and wandered up and down in the wood, seeking some little shelter from the cold wind and rain.
3:3	F:Sa	S:S	Presently a fox met him: "What"s the matter, my friend?" said he, "why do you hang down your head and look so lonely and woe-begone?"
4:4	Sa:Sa	S:S	"Ah!" replied the horse, "justice and avarice never dwell in one house; my master has forgotten all that I have done for him so many years, and because I can no longer work he has turned me adrift, and says unless I become stronger than a lion he will not take me back again; what chance can I have of that? he knows I have none, or he would not talk so."
5:5	N:H	N:H	However, the fox bid him be of good cheer, and said, "I will help you; lie down there, stretch yourself out quite stiff, and pretend to be dead."
6:6	N:N	N:N	The horse did as he was told, and the fox went straight to the lion who lived in a cave close by, and said to him, "A little way off lies a dead horse; come with me and you may make an excellent meal of his carcase."
7:7	H:H	N:H	The lion was greatly pleased, and set off immediately; and when they came to the horse, the fox said, "You will not be able to eat him comfortably here; I"ll tell you what--I will tie you fast to his tail, and then you can draw him to your den, and eat him at your leisure."
8:8	H:H	N:H	This advice pleased the lion, so he laid himself down quietly for the fox to make him fast to the horse.
9:9	N:N	N:N	But the fox managed to tie his legs together and bound all so hard and fast that with all his strength he could not set himself free.
10:10	N:N	N:N	When the work was done, the fox clapped the horse on the shoulder, and said, "Jip!
11:11	N:N	N:N	Dobbin!
12:12	N:N	N:N	Jip!"

14:14	A:A	N:A	The beast began to roar and bellow, till all the birds of the wood flew away for fright; but the horse let him sing on, and made his way quietly over the fields to his master"s house.
15:15	H:H	H:H	"Here he is, master," said he, "I have got the better of him": and when the farmer saw his old servant, his heart relented, and he said.
16:16	N:H	H:H	"Thou shalt stay in thy stable and be well taken care of."
17:17	N:H	H:H	And so the poor old horse had plenty to eat, and lived--till he died.
0:0	N:N	N:N	One fine evening a young princess put on her bonnet and clogs, and went out to take a walk by herself in a wood; and when she came to a cool spring of water, that rose in the midst of it, she sat herself down to rest a while.
1:1	H:N	H:N	Now she had a golden ball in her hand, which was her favourite plaything; and she was always tossing it up into the air, and catching it again as it fell.
2:2	N:N	N:N	After a time she threw it up so high that she missed catching it as it fell; and the ball bounded away, and rolled along upon the ground, till at last it fell down into the spring.
3:3	N:N	N:N	The princess looked into the spring after her ball, but it was very deep, so deep that she could not see the bottom of it.
4:4	Sa:Sa	S:N	Then she began to bewail her loss, and said, "Alas! if I could only get my ball again, I would give all my fine clothes and jewels, and everything that I have in the world."
5:5	N:Sa	N:N	Whilst she was speaking, a frog put its head out of the water, and said, "Princess, why do you weep so bitterly?"
6:6	Sa:Sa	S:N	"Alas!" said she, "what can you do for me, you nasty frog?
7:7	Sa:N	S:N	My golden ball has fallen into the spring."
8:8	H:H	N:N	The frog said, "I want not your pearls, and jewels, and fine clothes; but if you will love me, and let me live with you and eat from off your golden plate, and sleep upon your bed, I will bring you your ball again."
9:9	D:A	N:N	"What nonsense," thought the princess, "this silly frog is talking!
10:10	H:H	N:N	He can never even get out of the spring to visit me, though he may be able to get my ball for me, and therefore I will tell him he shall have what he asks."
11:11	N:H	N:N	So she said to the frog, "Well, if you will bring me my ball, I will do all you ask."
12:12	N:N	N:N	Then the frog put his head down, and dived deep under the water; and after a little while he came up again, with the ball in his mouth, and threw it on the edge of the spring.

15:15	N:N	N:-	The next day, just as the princess had sat down to dinner, she heard a strange noise--tap, tap--plash, plash--as if something was coming up the marble staircase: and soon afterwards there was a gentle knock at the door, and a little voice cried out and said:
16:16	H:H	N:+	"Open the door, my princess dear, Open the door to thy true love here!
17:17	N:N	N:N	And mind the words that thou and I said By the fountain cool, in the greenwood shade."
18:18	Su-:N	-:N	Then the princess ran to the door and opened it, and there she saw the frog, whom she had quite forgotten.
19:19	Su+:D	-:-	At this sight she was sadly frightened, and shutting the door as fast as she could came back to her seat.
20:20	Su-:N	-:N	The king, her father, seeing that something had frightened her, asked her what was the matter.
21:21	D:Sa	-:N	"There is a nasty frog," said she, "at the door, that lifted my ball for me out of the spring this morning: I told him that he should live with me here, thinking that he could never get out of the spring; but there he is at the door, and he wants to come in."
22:22	N:N	-:N	While she was speaking the frog knocked again at the door, and said:
23:23	N:A	N:-	"Open the door, my princess dear, Open the door to thy true love here!

29:29	N:N	N:H	This she did, and when he had eaten as much as he could, he said, "Now I am tired; carry me upstairs, and put me into your bed."
30:30	D:A	-:N	And the princess, though very unwilling, took him up in her hand, and put him upon the pillow of her own bed, where he slept all night long.

39:39	H:N	H:N	He told her that he had been enchanted by a spiteful fairy, who had changed him into a frog; and that he had been fated so to abide till some princess should take him out of the spring, and let him eat from her plate, and sleep upon her bed for three nights.
40:40	H:H	H:+	"You," said the prince, "have broken his cruel charm, and now I have nothing to wish for but that you should go with me into my father"s kingdom, where I will marry you, and love you as long as you live."
41:41	H:H	H:+	The young princess, you may be sure, was not long in saying "Yes" to all this; and as they spoke a gay coach drove up, with eight beautiful horses, decked with plumes of feathers and a golden harness; and behind the coach rode the prince"s servant, faithful Heinrich, who had bewailed the misfortunes of his dear master during his enchantment so long and so bitterly, that his heart had well-nigh burst.
42:42	H:H	H:H	They then took leave of the king, and got into the coach with eight horses, and all set out, full of joy and merriment, for the prince"s kingdom, which they reached safely; and there they lived happily a great many years.
0:0	N:N	N:N	A certain king had a beautiful garden, and in the garden stood a tree which bore golden apples.
1:1	N:N	N:N	These apples were always counted, and about the time when they began to grow ripe it was found that every night one of them was gone.
2:2	A:A	A:N	The king became very angry at this, and ordered the gardener to keep watch all night under the tree.
3:3	N:N	N:N	The gardener set his eldest son to watch; but about twelve o"clock he fell asleep, and in the morning another of the apples was missing.
4:4	N:N	N:N	Then the second son was ordered to watch; and at midnight he too fell asleep, and in the morning another apple was gone.
5:5	F:F	F:N	Then the third son offered to keep watch; but the gardener at first would not let him, for fear some harm should come to him: however, at last he consented, and the young man laid himself under the tree to watch.

7:7	N:Su-	N:+	But the arrow did the bird no harm; only it dropped a golden feather from its tail, and then flew away.
8:8	N:N	N:N	The golden feather was brought to the king in the morning, and all the council was called together.
9:9	D:A	N:N	Everyone agreed that it was worth more than all the wealth of the kingdom: but the king said, "One feather is of no use to me, I must have the whole bird."
10:10	N:N	N:N	Then the gardener"s eldest son set out and thought to find the golden bird very easily; and when he had gone but a little way, he came to a wood, and by the side of the wood he saw a fox sitting; so he took his bow and made ready to shoot at it.
11:11	F:N	N:N	Then the fox said, "Do not shoot me, for I will give you good counsel; I know what your business is, and that you want to find the golden bird.
12:12	N:N	N:N	You will reach a village in the evening; and when you get there, you will see two inns opposite to each other, one of which is very pleasant and beautiful to look at: go not in there, but rest for the night in the other, though it may appear to you to be very poor and mean."
13:13	D:Su-	D:-	But the son thought to himself, "What can such a beast as this know about the matter?"
14:14	N:N	N:-	So he shot his arrow at the fox; but he missed it, and it set up its tail above its back and ran into the wood.
15:15	N:N	N:N	Then he went his way, and in the evening came to the village where the two inns were; and in one of these were people singing, and dancing, and feasting; but the other looked very dirty, and poor.
16:16	H:H	N:-	"I should be very silly," said he, "if I went to that shabby house, and left this charming place"; so he went into the smart house, and ate and drank at his ease, and forgot the bird, and his country too.
17:17	N:N	N:-	Time passed on; and as the eldest son did not come back, and no tidings were heard of him, the second son set out, and the same thing happened to him.
18:18	H:N	H:-	He met the fox, who gave him the good advice: but when he came to the two inns, his eldest brother was standing at the window where the merrymaking was, and called to him to come in; and he could not withstand the temptation, but went in, and forgot the golden bird and his country in the same manner.
19:19	F:F	N:N	Time passed on again, and the youngest son too wished to set out into the wide world to seek for the golden bird; but his father would not listen to it for a long while, for he was very fond of his son, and was afraid that some ill luck might happen to him also, and prevent his coming back.
20:20	N:N	N:N	However, at last it was agreed he should go, for he would not rest at home; and as he came to the wood, he met the fox, and heard the same good counsel.
21:21	H:H	H:N	But he was thankful to the fox, and did not attempt his life as his brothers had done; so the fox said, "Sit upon my tail, and you will travel faster."

23:23	N:H	N:N	When they came to the village, the son followed the fox"s counsel, and without looking about him went to the shabby inn and rested there all night at his ease.
24:24	N:N	N:N	In the morning came the fox again and met him as he was beginning his journey, and said, "Go straight forward, till you come to a castle, before which lie a whole troop of soldiers fast asleep and snoring: take no notice of them, but go into the castle and pass on and on till you come to a room, where the golden bird sits in a wooden cage; close by it stands a beautiful golden cage; but do not try to take the bird out of the shabby cage and put it into the handsome one, otherwise you will repent it."

0:0	Sa:N	S:N	There was a man who had three sons, the youngest of whom was called Dummling,[*] and was despised, mocked, and sneered at on every occasion.
1:1	N:N	S:N	It happened that the eldest wanted to go into the forest to hew wood, and before he went his mother gave him a beautiful sweet cake and a bottle of wine in order that he might not suffer from hunger or thirst.
2:2	Sa:N	S:N	When he entered the forest he met a little grey-haired old man who bade him good day, and said: "Do give me a piece of cake out of your pocket, and let me have a draught of your wine; I am so hungry and thirsty."
3:3	A:A	S:N	But the clever son answered: "If I give you my cake and wine, I shall have none for myself; be off with you," and he left the little man standing and went on.
4:4	F:N	S:-	But when he began to hew down a tree, it was not long before he made a false stroke, and the axe cut him in the arm, so that he had to go home and have it bound up.
5:5	Su-:N	S:-	And this was the little grey man"s doing.
6:6	N:N	S:N	After this the second son went into the forest, and his mother gave him, like the eldest, a cake and a bottle of wine.

9:9	F:N	S:-	His punishment, however, was not delayed; when he had made a few blows at the tree he struck himself in the leg, so that he had to be carried home.
10:10	Sa:N	S:N	Then Dummling said: "Father, do let me go and cut wood."
11:11	F:N	F:N	The father answered: "Your brothers have hurt themselves with it, leave it alone, you do not understand anything about it."
12:12	Sa:N	F:A	But Dummling begged so long that at last he said: "Just go then, you will get wiser by hurting yourself."
13:13	N:N	F:N	His mother gave him a cake made with water and baked in the cinders, and with it a bottle of sour beer.
14:14	Sa:N	S:N	When he came to the forest the little old grey man met him likewise, and greeting him, said: "Give me a piece of your cake and a drink out of your bottle; I am so hungry and thirsty."
15:15	Sa:N	S:H	Dummling answered: "I have only cinder-cake and sour beer; if that pleases you, we will sit down and eat."
16:16	Su+:N	+:H	So they sat down, and when Dummling pulled out his cinder-cake, it was a fine sweet cake, and the sour beer had become good wine.
17:17	H:N	+:H	So they ate and drank, and after that the little man said: "Since you have a good heart, and are willing to divide what you have, I will give you good luck.
18:18	N:N	+:H	There stands an old tree, cut it down, and you will find something at the roots."
19:19	N:N	+:N	Then the little man took leave of him.
20:20	Su+:N	+:H	Dummling went and cut down the tree, and when it fell there was a goose sitting in the roots with feathers of pure gold.
21:21	H:N	H:N	He lifted her up, and taking her with him, went to an inn where he thought he would stay the night.
22:22	N:N	H:N	Now the host had three daughters, who saw the goose and were curious to know what such a wonderful bird might be, and would have liked to have one of its golden feathers.
23:23	Su-:N	H:-	The eldest thought: "I shall soon find an opportunity of pulling out a feather," and as soon as Dummling had gone out she seized the goose by the wing, but her finger and hand remained sticking fast to it.
24:24	Su-:N	H:-	The second came soon afterwards, thinking only of how she might get a feather for herself, but she had scarcely touched her sister than she was held fast.
25:25	A:F	A:-	At last the third also came with the like intent, and the others screamed out: "Keep away; for goodness" sake keep away!"
26:26	Sa:N	A:N	But she did not understand why she was to keep away.
27:27	A:N	A:-	"The others are there," she thought, "I may as well be there too," and ran to them; but as soon as she had touched her sister, she remained sticking fast to her.
28:28	A:N	A:N	So they had to spend the night with the goose.
29:29	N:N	F:+	The next morning Dummling took the goose under his arm and set out, without troubling himself about the three girls who were hanging on to it.
30:30	F:N	F:+	They were obliged to run after him continually, now left, now right, wherever his legs took him.
31:31	A:A	F:+	In the middle of the fields the parson met them, and when he saw the procession he said: "For shame, you good-for-nothing girls, why are you running across the fields after this young man?
32:32	A:N	F:+	Is that seemly?"
33:33	Su-:N	F:+	At the same time he seized the youngest by the hand in order to pull her away, but as soon as he touched her he likewise stuck fast, and was himself obliged to run behind.
34:34	Su-:N	F:+	Before long the sexton came by and saw his master, the parson, running behind three girls.
35:35	Su-:Su+	F:+	He was astonished at this and called out: "Hi! your reverence, whither away so quickly?
36:36	Su-:N	F:+	Do not forget that we have a christening today!" and running after him he took him by the sleeve, but was also held fast to it.
37:37	F:F	F:+	Whilst the five were trotting thus one behind the other, two labourers came with their hoes from the fields; the parson called out to them and begged that they would set him and the sexton free.
38:38	Su-:N	F:+	But they had scarcely touched the sexton when they were held fast, and now there were seven of them running behind Dummling and the goose.
39:39	N:N	F:+	Soon afterwards he came to a city, where a king ruled who had a daughter who was so serious that no one could make her laugh.
40:40	N:N	F:+	So he had put forth a decree that whosoever should be able to make her laugh should marry her.
41:41	H:H	H:+	When Dummling heard this, he went with his goose and all her train before the king"s daughter, and as soon as she saw the seven people running on and on, one behind the other, she began to laugh quite loudly, and as if she would never stop.
42:42	F:N	H:-	Thereupon Dummling asked to have her for his wife; but the king did not like the son-inlaw, and made all manner of excuses and said he must first produce a man who could drink a cellarful of wine.
43:43	Su-:N	F:N	Dummling thought of the little grey man, who could certainly help him; so he went into the forest, and in the same place where he had felled the tree, he saw a man sitting, who had a very sorrowful face.
44:44	Sa:Sa	F:N	Dummling asked him what he was taking to heart so sorely, and he answered: "I have such a great thirst and cannot quench it; cold water I cannot stand, a barrel of wine I have just emptied, but that to me is like a drop on a hot stone!"
45:45	H:N	F:N	"There, I can help you," said Dummling, "just come with me and you shall be satisfied."
46:46	H:N	H:H	He led him into the king"s cellar, and the man bent over the huge barrels, and drank and drank till his loins hurt, and before the day was out he had emptied all the barrels.
47:47	A:A	F:-	Then Dummling asked once more for his bride, but the king was vexed that such an ugly fellow, whom everyone called Dummling, should take away his daughter, and he made a new condition; he must first find a man who could eat a whole mountain of bread.
48:48	Sa:N	F:N	Dummling did not think long, but went straight into the forest, where in the same place there sat a man who was tying up his body with a strap, and making an awful face, and saying: "I have eaten a whole ovenful of rolls, but what good is that when one has such a hunger as I?
49:49	Sa:Sa	F:N	My stomach remains empty, and I must tie myself up if I am not to die of hunger."
50:50	H:H	F:H	At this Dummling was glad, and said: "Get up and come with me; you shall eat yourself full."
51:51	N:N	F:N	He led him to the king"s palace where all the flour in the whole Kingdom was collected, and from it he caused a huge mountain of bread to be baked.
52:52	H:N	F:+	The man from the forest stood before it, began to eat, and by the end of one day the whole mountain had vanished.
53:53	A:N	F:N	Then Dummling for the third time asked for his bride; but the king again sought a way out, and ordered a ship which could sail on land and on water.
54:54	A:N	F:N	"As soon as you come sailing back in it," said he, "you shall have my daughter for wife."
55:55	Su+:N	F:N	Dummling went straight into the forest, and there sat the little grey man to whom he had given his cake.
56:56	H:N	H:N	When he heard what Dummling wanted, he said: "Since you have given me to eat and to drink, I will give you the ship; and I do all this because you once were kind to me."
57:57	A:N	H:H	Then he gave him the ship which could sail on land and water, and when the king saw that, he could no longer prevent him from having his daughter.
58:58	H:H	H:H	The wedding was celebrated, and after the king"s death, Dummling inherited his kingdom and lived for a long time contentedly with his wife.
59:59	N:N	H:N	[*] Simpleton
0:0	N:N	N:N	Long, long ago, some two thousand years or so, there lived a rich man with a good and beautiful wife.
1:1	Sa:Sa	S:S	They loved each other dearly, but sorrowed much that they had no children.
2:2	N:Sa	N:S	So greatly did they desire to have one, that the wife prayed for it day and night, but still they remained childless.
3:3	N:N	N:N	In front of the house there was a court, in which grew a juniper-tree.
4:4	N:N	N:N	One winter"s day the wife stood under the tree to peel some apples, and as she was peeling them, she cut her finger, and the blood fell on the snow.
5:5	H:H	H:H	"Ah," sighed the woman heavily, "if I had but a child, as red as blood and as white as snow," and as she spoke the words, her heart grew light within her, and it seemed to her that her wish was granted, and she returned to the house feeling glad and comforted.
6:6	N:N	N:N	A month passed, and the snow had all disappeared; then another month went by, and all the earth was green.
7:7	N:N	N:N	So the months followed one another, and first the trees budded in the woods, and soon the green branches grew thickly intertwined, and then the blossoms began to fall.
8:8	H:H	H:H	Once again the wife stood under the juniper-tree, and it was so full of sweet scent that her heart leaped for joy, and she was so overcome with her happiness, that she fell on her knees.
9:9	Sa:Sa	S:S	Presently the fruit became round and firm, and she was glad and at peace; but when they were fully ripe she picked the berries and ate eagerly of them, and then she grew sad and ill.
10:10	Sa:Sa	S:S	A little while later she called her husband, and said to him, weeping.
11:11	Sa:Sa	S:S	"If I die, bury me under the juniper-tree."
12:12	H:H	H:S	Then she felt comforted and happy again, and before another month had passed she had a little child, and when she saw that it was as white as snow and as red as blood, her joy was so great that she died.
13:13	Sa:Sa	S:S	Her husband buried her under the juniper-tree, and wept bitterly for her.
14:14	Sa:Sa	S:S	By degrees, however, his sorrow grew less, and although at times he still grieved over his loss, he was able to go about as usual, and later on he married again.
15:15	N:H	N:H	He now had a little daughter born to him; the child of his first wife was a boy, who was as red as blood and as white as snow.
16:16	A:A	A:N	The mother loved her daughter very much, and when she looked at her and then looked at the boy, it pierced her heart to think that he would always stand in the way of her own child, and she was continually thinking how she could get the whole of the property for her.
17:17	A:A	A:N	This evil thought took possession of her more and more, and made her behave very unkindly to the boy.
18:18	F:F	F:S	She drove him from place to place with cuffings and buffetings, so that the poor child went about in fear, and had no peace from the time he left school to the time he went back.
19:19	N:N	N:S	One day the little daughter came running to her mother in the storeroom, and said, "Mother, give me an apple."
20:20	N:N	N:S	"Yes, my child," said the wife, and she gave her a beautiful apple out of the chest; the chest had a very heavy lid and a large iron lock.
21:21	N:Su-	N:S	"Mother," said the little daughter again, "may not brother have one too?"

34:34	F:F	F:S	Soon after this, little Marleen came up to her mother who was stirring a pot of boiling water over the fire, and said, "Mother, brother is sitting by the door with an apple in his hand, and he looks so pale; and when I asked him to give me the apple, he did not answer, and that frightened me."

0:0	N:N	N:N	There was once a merchant who had only one child, a son, that was very young, and barely able to run alone.
1:1	N:N	N:S	He had two richly laden ships then making a voyage upon the seas, in which he had embarked all his wealth, in the hope of making great gains, when the news came that both were lost.
2:2	F:Sa	F:S	Thus from being a rich man he became all at once so very poor that nothing was left to him but one small plot of land; and there he often went in an evening to take his walk, and ease his mind of a little of his trouble.
3:3	Su-:Sa	-:S	One day, as he was roaming along in a brown study, thinking with no great comfort on what he had been and what he now was, and was like to be, all on a sudden there stood before him a little, rough-looking, black dwarf.
4:4	N:Sa	N:S	"Prithee, friend, why so sorrowful?" said he to the merchant; "what is it you take so deeply to heart?"

6:6	N:N	N:N	"Who knows but I may?" said the little man: "tell me what ails you, and perhaps you will find I may be of some use."
7:7	N:N	N:S	Then the merchant told him how all his wealth was gone to the bottom of the sea, and how he had nothing left but that little plot of land.
8:8	N:N	N:N	"Oh, trouble not yourself about that," said the dwarf; "only undertake to bring me here, twelve years hence, whatever meets you first on your going home, and I will give you as much as you please."
9:9	N:N	N:N	The merchant thought this was no great thing to ask; that it would most likely be his dog or his cat, or something of that sort, but forgot his little boy Heinel; so he agreed to the bargain, and signed and sealed the bond to do what was asked of him.
10:10	H:H	H:H	But as he drew near home, his little boy was so glad to see him that he crept behind him, and laid fast hold of his legs, and looked up in his face and laughed.
11:11	F:F	F:F	Then the father started, trembling with fear and horror, and saw what it was that he had bound himself to do; but as no gold was come, he made himself easy by thinking that it was only a joke that the dwarf was playing him, and that, at any rate, when the money came, he should see the bearer, and would not take it in.
12:12	N:N	N:+	About a month afterwards he went upstairs into a lumber-room to look for some old iron, that he might sell it and raise a little money; and there, instead of his iron, he saw a large pile of gold lying on the floor.
13:13	H:H	H:H	At the sight of this he was overjoyed, and forgetting all about his son, went into trade again, and became a richer merchant than before.
14:14	Sa:Sa	S:S	Meantime little Heinel grew up, and as the end of the twelve years drew near the merchant began to call to mind his bond, and became very sad and thoughtful; so that care and sorrow were written upon his face.
15:15	N:N	N:S	The boy one day asked what was the matter, but his father would not tell for some time; at last, however, he said that he had, without knowing it, sold him for gold to a little, ugly-looking, black dwarf, and that the twelve years were coming round when he must keep his word.
16:16	N:N	N:S	Then Heinel said, "Father, give yourself very little trouble about that; I shall be too much for the little man."

18:18	N:F	N:F	The little black dwarf soon came, and walked round and round about the circle, but could not find any way to get into it, and he either could not, or dared not, jump over it.
19:19	N:N	N:N	At last the boy said to him.

22:22	N:N	N:N	"Have you brought me what you said you would?" said the dwarf to the merchant.

0:0	N:N	N:N	Once upon a time, a mouse, a bird, and a sausage, entered into partnership and set up house together.
1:1	H:N	H:N	For a long time all went well; they lived in great comfort, and prospered so far as to be able to add considerably to their stores.

3:3	F:N	H:N	When people are too well off they always begin to long for something new.

8:8	N:N	F:H	Then, when the bird came home and had laid aside his burden, they sat down to table, and when they had finished their meal, they could sleep their fill till the following morning: and that was really a very delightful life.

0:0	N:N	N:N	Two kings" sons once upon a time went into the world to seek their fortunes; but they soon fell into a wasteful foolish way of living, so that they could not return home again.
1:1	D:D	D:D	Then their brother, who was a little insignificant dwarf, went out to seek for his brothers: but when he had found them they only laughed at him, to think that he, who was so young and simple, should try to travel through the world, when they, who were so much wiser, had been unable to get on.
2:2	N:N	N:N	However, they all set out on their journey together, and came at last to an anthill.

16:16	N:N	N:N	The eldest brother set out, and sought for the pearls the whole day: but the evening came, and he had not found the first hundred: so he was turned into stone as the tablet had foretold.

18:18	Su+:N	F:N	Meantime the huntsman came nearer and looked at the lady, and said to himself, "I have been travelling so long that I should like to go into this castle and rest myself, for I have money enough to pay for anything I want"; but the real reason was, that he wanted to see more of the beautiful lady.
19:19	H:H	F:H	Then he went into the house, and was welcomed kindly; and it was not long before he was so much in love that he thought of nothing else but looking at the lady"s eyes, and doing everything that she wished.

31:31	Sa:Sa	S:S	When he awoke and found that his lady had tricked him, and left him alone on the wild rock, he said, "Alas! what roguery there is in the world!" and there he sat in great grief and fear, not knowing what to do.
32:32	F:N	F:N	Now this rock belonged to fierce giants who lived upon it; and as he saw three of them striding about, he thought to himself, "I can only save myself by feigning to be asleep"; so he laid himself down as if he were in a sound sleep.

36:36	N:N	F:N	And they passed on.
37:37	F:N	F:N	But the huntsman had heard all they said; and as soon as they were gone, he climbed to the top of the mountain, and when he had sat there a short time a cloud came rolling around him, and caught him in a whirlwind and bore him along for some time, till it settled in a garden, and he fell quite gently to the ground amongst the greens and cabbages.
38:38	Sa:N	F:S	Then he looked around him, and said, "I wish I had something to eat, if not I shall be worse off than before; for here I see neither apples nor pears, nor any kind of fruits, nothing but vegetables."

42:42	H:N	H:S	Then he laid himself down and slept off a little of his weariness; and when he awoke the next morning he broke off a head both of the good and the bad salad, and thought to himself, "This will help me to my fortune again, and enable me to pay off some folks for their treachery."
43:43	H:N	H:H	So he went away to try and find the castle of his friends; and after wandering about a few days he luckily found it.

64:64	H:N	H:H	After this he went back to the castle, where he found everything he wanted.

0:0	N:N	N:N	There was once a man who had seven sons, and last of all one daughter.
1:1	Sa:N	F:S	Although the little girl was very pretty, she was so weak and small that they thought she could not live; but they said she should at once be christened.
2:2	F:N	F:N	So the father sent one of his sons in haste to the spring to get some water, but the other six ran with him.
3:3	F:F	F:F	Each wanted to be first at drawing the water, and so they were in such a hurry that all let their pitchers fall into the well, and they stood very foolishly looking at one another, and did not know what to do, for none dared go home.
4:4	F:A	F:F	In the meantime the father was uneasy, and could not tell what made the young men stay so long.
5:5	A:A	F:A	"Surely," said he, "the whole seven must have forgotten themselves over some game of play"; and when he had waited still longer and they yet did not come, he flew into a rage and wished them all turned into ravens.
6:6	N:N	F:-	Scarcely had he spoken these words when he heard a croaking over his head, and looked up and saw seven ravens as black as coal flying round and round.
7:7	Sa:Sa	F:S	Sorry as he was to see his wish so fulfilled, he did not know how what was done could be undone, and comforted himself as well as he could for the loss of his seven sons with his dear little daughter, who soon became stronger and every day more beautiful.
8:8	Su-:N	S:N	For a long time she did not know that she had ever had any brothers; for her father and mother took care not to speak of them before her: but one day by chance she heard the people about her speak of them.

11:11	Sa:Sa	S:S	So they dared no longer hide the truth from her, but said it was the will of Heaven, and that her birth was only the innocent cause of it; but the little girl mourned sadly about it every day, and thought herself bound to do all she could to bring her brothers back; and she had neither rest nor ease, till at length one day she stole away, and set out into the wide world to find her brothers, wherever they might be, and free them, whatever it might cost her.
12:12	F:N	S:N	She took nothing with her but a little ring which her father and mother had given her, a loaf of bread in case she should be hungry, a little pitcher of water in case she should be thirsty, and a little stool to rest upon when she should be weary.
13:13	F:N	F:N	Thus she went on and on, and journeyed till she came to the world"s end; then she came to the sun, but the sun looked much too hot and fiery; so she ran away quickly to the moon, but the moon was cold and chilly, and said, "I smell flesh and blood this way!" so she took herself away in a hurry and came to the stars, and the stars were friendly and kind to her, and each star sat upon his own little stool; but the morning star rose up and gave her a little piece of wood, and said, "If you have not this little piece of wood, you cannot unlock the castle that stands on the glass-mountain, and there your brothers live."
14:14	F:N	F:N	The little girl took the piece of wood, rolled it up in a little cloth, and went on again until she came to the glass-mountain, and found the door shut.
15:15	Sa:N	F:S	Then she felt for the little piece of wood; but when she unwrapped the cloth it was not there, and she saw she had lost the gift of the good stars.
16:16	N:N	F:F	What was to be done?
17:17	Sa:N	F:-	She wanted to save her brothers, and had no key of the castle of the glass-mountain; so this faithful little sister took a knife out of her pocket and cut off her little finger, that was just the size of the piece of wood she had lost, and put it in the door and opened it.
18:18	N:N	F:-	As she went in, a little dwarf came up to her, and said, "What are you seeking for?"

0:0	N:N	N:N	A certain father had two sons, the elder of who was smart and sensible, and could do everything, but the younger was stupid and could neither learn nor understand anything, and when people saw him they said: "There"s a fellow who will give his father some trouble!"

28:28	N:N	N:F	"Well, Donkey," said Chanticleer, "what do you see?"

33:33	N:N	N:N	The ass placed himself upright on his hind legs, with his forefeet resting against the window; the dog got upon his back; the cat scrambled up to the dog"s shoulders, and the cock flew up and sat upon the cat"s head.
34:34	N:N	N:N	When all was ready a signal was given, and they began their music.
35:35	Su+:N	N:-	The ass brayed, the dog barked, the cat mewed, and the cock screamed; and then they all broke through the window at once, and came tumbling into the room, amongst the broken glass, with a most hideous clatter!

37:37	H:H	H:H	The coast once clear, our travellers soon sat down and dispatched what the robbers had left, with as much eagerness as if they had not expected to eat again for a month.
38:38	H:N	H:H	As soon as they had satisfied themselves, they put out the lights, and each once more sought out a resting-place to his own liking.
39:39	N:N	N:H	The donkey laid himself down upon a heap of straw in the yard, the dog stretched himself upon a mat behind the door, the cat rolled herself up on the hearth before the warm ashes, and the cock perched upon a beam on the top of the house; and, as they were all rather tired with their journey, they soon fell asleep.
40:40	N:A	N:F	But about midnight, when the robbers saw from afar that the lights were out and that all seemed quiet, they began to think that they had been in too great a hurry to run away; and one of them, who was bolder than the rest, went to see what was going on.

0:0	N:N	N:N	There were two brothers who were both soldiers; the one was rich and the other poor.
1:1	N:N	N:N	The poor man thought he would try to better himself; so, pulling off his red coat, he became a gardener, and dug his ground well, and sowed turnips.

3:3	Su+:Su+	N:N	At last it was so big that it filled a cart, and two oxen could hardly draw it; and the gardener knew not what in the world to do with it, nor whether it would be a blessing or a curse to him.

28:28	N:H	N:+	Here I discern the signs and motions of the heavens and the stars; the laws that control the winds; the number of the sands on the seashore; the healing of the sick; the virtues of all simples, of birds, and of precious stones.

10:10	H:N	H:N	In the meantime the smell of the sweet jam rose to where the flies were sitting in great numbers, and they were attracted and descended on it in hosts.
11:11	H:N	H:A	"Hi! who invited you?" said the little tailor, and drove the unbidden guests away.
12:12	N:N	H:N	The flies, however, who understood no German, would not be turned away, but came back again in ever-increasing companies.
13:13	A:A	H:A	The little tailor at last lost all patience, and drew a piece of cloth from the hole under his work-table, and saying: "Wait, and I will give it to you," struck it mercilessly on them.

16:16	H:Su+	H:+	"The whole town shall know of this!"
17:17	H:N	H:+	And the little tailor hastened to cut himself a girdle, stitched it, and embroidered on it in large letters: "Seven at one stroke!"

19:19	H:N	H:N	The tailor put on the girdle, and resolved to go forth into the world, because he thought his workshop was too small for his valour.
20:20	N:N	H:N	Before he went away, he sought about in the house to see if there was anything which he could take with him; however, he found nothing but an old cheese, and that he put in his pocket.
21:21	N:N	H:N	In front of the door he observed a bird which had caught itself in the thicket.
22:22	N:N	H:N	It had to go into his pocket with the cheese.
23:23	H:N	H:N	Now he took to the road boldly, and as he was light and nimble, he felt no fatigue.
24:24	H:N	H:N	The road led him up a mountain, and when he had reached the highest point of it, there sat a powerful giant looking peacefully about him.

27:27	N:N	H:H	Have you any inclination to go with me?"

39:39	H:N	H:H	The bird, delighted with its liberty, rose, flew away and did not come back.

112:112	F:N	H:N	The little tailor demanded of the king the promised reward; he, however, repented of his promise, and again bethought himself how he could get rid of the hero.
