143:143	N:N	H:H	So the little tailor was and remained a king to the end of his life.
0:0	N:N	N:N	Long before you or I were born, there reigned, in a country a great way off, a king who had three sons.
1:1	Sa:N	N:N	This king once fell very ill--so ill that nobody thought he could live.
2:2	Sa:Sa	S:S	His sons were very much grieved at their father"s sickness; and as they were walking together very mournfully in the garden of the palace, a little old man met them and asked what was the matter.
3:3	Sa:Sa	S:S	They told him that their father was very ill, and that they were afraid nothing could save him.
4:4	H:N	N:S	"I know what would," said the little old man; "it is the Water of Life.
5:5	N:N	N:S	If he could have a draught of it he would be well again; but it is very hard to get."
6:6	F:N	S:S	Then the eldest son said, "I will soon find it": and he went to the sick king, and begged that he might go in search of the Water of Life, as it was the only thing that could save him.
7:7	N:N	S:S	"No," said the king.
8:8	D:F	S:S	"I had rather die than place you in such great danger as you must meet with in your journey."
9:9	H:H	N:S	But he begged so hard that the king let him go; and the prince thought to himself, "If I bring my father this water, he will make me sole heir to his kingdom."
10:10	F:Su-	N:N	Then he set out: and when he had gone on his way some time he came to a deep valley, overhung with rocks and woods; and as he looked around, he saw standing above him on one of the rocks a little ugly dwarf, with a sugarloaf cap and a scarlet cloak; and the dwarf called to him and said, "Prince, whither so fast?"
11:11	D:A	D:-	"What is that to thee, you ugly imp?" said the prince haughtily, and rode on.
12:12	A:A	A:N	But the dwarf was enraged at his behaviour, and laid a fairy spell of ill-luck upon him; so that as he rode on the mountain pass became narrower and narrower, and at last the way was so straitened that he could not go to step forward: and when he thought to have turned his horse round and go back the way he came, he heard a loud laugh ringing round him, and found that the path was closed behind him, so that he was shut in all round.
13:13	Su-:F	-:N	He next tried to get off his horse and make his way on foot, but again the laugh rang in his ears, and he found himself unable to move a step, and thus he was forced to abide spellbound.
14:14	F:N	N:N	Meantime the old king was lingering on in daily hope of his son"s return, till at last the second son said, "Father, I will go in search of the Water of Life."
15:15	H:H	N:N	For he thought to himself, "My brother is surely dead, and the kingdom will fall to me if I find the water."
16:16	N:A	N:N	The king was at first very unwilling to let him go, but at last yielded to his wish.
17:17	N:Su-	N:N	So he set out and followed the same road which his brother had done, and met with the same elf, who stopped him at the same spot in the mountains, saying, as before, "Prince, prince, whither so fast?"
18:18	D:A	D:A	"Mind your own affairs, busybody!" said the prince scornfully, and rode on.
19:19	N:N	D:A	But the dwarf put the same spell upon him as he put on his elder brother, and he, too, was at last obliged to take up his abode in the heart of the mountains.
20:20	N:N	N:A	Thus it is with proud silly people, who think themselves above everyone else, and are too proud to ask or take advice.
21:21	N:H	N:H	When the second prince had thus been gone a long time, the youngest son said he would go and search for the Water of Life, and trusted he should soon be able to make his father well again.
22:22	N:N	N:N	So he set out, and the dwarf met him too at the same spot in the valley, among the mountains, and said, "Prince, whither so fast?"
23:23	N:Su+	N:N	And the prince said, "I am going in search of the Water of Life, because my father is ill, and like to die: can you help me?
24:24	F:H	N:+	Pray be kind, and aid me if you can!"
25:25	N:N	N:+	"Do you know where it is to be found?" asked the dwarf.
26:26	N:N	N:+	"No," said the prince, "I do not.
27:27	N:H	N:+	Pray tell me if you know."
28:28	H:H	N:+	"Then as you have spoken to me kindly, and are wise enough to seek for advice, I will tell you how and where to go.
29:29	N:N	N:F	The water you seek springs from a well in an enchanted castle; and, that you may be able to reach it in safety, I will give you an iron wand and two little loaves of bread; strike the iron door of the castle three times with the wand, and it will open: two hungry lions will be lying down inside gaping for their prey, but if you throw them the bread they will let you pass; then hasten on to the well, and take some of the Water of Life before the clock strikes twelve; for if you tarry longer the door will shut upon you for ever."
30:30	H:N	N:F	Then the prince thanked his little friend with the scarlet cloak for his friendly aid, and took the wand and the bread, and went travelling on and on, over sea and over land, till he came to his journey"s end, and found everything to be as the dwarf had told him.
31:31	N:N	N:F	The door flew open at the third stroke of the wand, and when the lions were quieted he went on through the castle and came at length to a beautiful hall.
32:32	N:N	N:F	Around it he saw several knights sitting in a trance; then he pulled off their rings and put them on his own fingers.
33:33	N:N	N:F	In another room he saw on a table a sword and a loaf of bread, which he also took.
34:34	H:H	H:+	Further on he came to a room where a beautiful young lady sat upon a couch; and she welcomed him joyfully, and said, if he would set her free from the spell that bound her, the kingdom should be his, if he would come back in a year and marry her.
35:35	N:N	N:+	Then she told him that the well that held the Water of Life was in the palace gardens; and bade him make haste, and draw what he wanted before the clock struck twelve.
36:36	N:N	N:F	He walked on; and as he walked through beautiful gardens he came to a delightful shady spot in which stood a couch; and he thought to himself, as he felt tired, that he would rest himself for a while, and gaze on the lovely scenes around him.
37:37	N:N	N:F	So he laid himself down, and sleep fell upon him unawares, so that he did not wake up till the clock was striking a quarter to twelve.
38:38	F:F	F:F	Then he sprang from the couch dreadfully frightened, ran to the well, filled a cup that was standing by him full of water, and hastened to get away in time.
39:39	Su-:N	-:F	Just as he was going out of the iron door it struck twelve, and the door fell so quickly upon him that it snapped off a piece of his heel.
40:40	Su+:H	H:H	When he found himself safe, he was overjoyed to think that he had got the Water of Life; and as he was going on his way homewards, he passed by the little dwarf, who, when he saw the sword and the loaf, said, "You have made a noble prize; with the sword you can at a blow slay whole armies, and the bread will never fail you."
41:41	N:Sa	N:H	Then the prince thought to himself, "I cannot go home to my father without my brothers"; so he said, "My dear friend, cannot you tell me where my two brothers are, who set out in search of the Water of Life before me, and never came back?"
42:42	N:A	N:H	"I have shut them up by a charm between two mountains," said the dwarf, "because they were proud and ill-behaved, and scorned to ask advice."

45:45	Su-:Sa	-:F	Then they all three rode on together, and on their way home came to a country that was laid waste by war and a dreadful famine, so that it was feared all must die for want.

0:0	N:N	N:N	A long time ago there lived a king who was famed for his wisdom through all the land.

38:38	N:N	N:S	She looked at him on every side and saw that something was moving and struggling in his gorged belly.
39:39	Su+:Su+	+:+	"Ah, heavens," she said, "is it possible that my poor children whom he has swallowed down for his supper, can be still alive?"
40:40	N:Su+	H:+	Then the kid had to run home and fetch scissors, and a needle and thread, and the goat cut open the monster"s stomach, and hardly had she made one cut, than one little kid thrust its head out, and when she had cut farther, all six sprang out one after another, and were all still alive, and had suffered no injury whatever, for in his greediness the monster had swallowed them down whole.
41:41	H:H	H:H	What rejoicing there was!
42:42	H:H	H:H	They embraced their dear mother, and jumped like a tailor at his wedding.
43:43	N:A	N:A	The mother, however, said: "Now go and look for some big stones, and we will fill the wicked beast"s stomach with them while he is still asleep."
44:44	N:N	N:N	Then the seven kids dragged the stones thither with all speed, and put as many of them into this stomach as they could get in; and the mother sewed him up again in the greatest haste, so that he was not aware of anything and never once stirred.
45:45	N:N	N:N	When the wolf at length had had his fill of sleep, he got on his legs, and as the stones in his stomach made him very thirsty, he wanted to go to a well to drink.
46:46	N:N	N:N	But when he began to walk and to move about, the stones in his stomach knocked against each other and rattled.
47:47	F:Su-	F:-	Then cried he:
48:48	F:Su-	F:-	"What rumbles and tumbles Against my poor bones?
49:49	N:N	F:-	I thought "twas six kids, But it feels like big stones."
50:50	Sa:Sa	H:S	And when he got to the well and stooped over the water to drink, the heavy stones made him fall in, and he drowned miserably.
51:51	H:H	H:H	When the seven kids saw that, they came running to the spot and cried aloud: "The wolf is dead!
52:52	H:H	H:H	The wolf is dead!" and danced for joy round about the well with their mother.
0:0	N:N	N:N	A poor woodman sat in his cottage one night, smoking his pipe by the fireside, while his wife sat by his side spinning.
1:1	Sa:Sa	S:S	"How lonely it is, wife," said he, as he puffed out a long curl of smoke, "for you and me to sit here by ourselves, without any children to play about and amuse us while other people seem so happy and merry with their children!"
2:2	Sa:Sa	S:S	"What you say is very true," said the wife, sighing, and turning round her wheel; "how happy should I be if I had but one child!
3:3	N:Sa	S:S	If it were ever so small--nay, if it were no bigger than my thumb--I should be very happy, and love it dearly."
4:4	N:Su+	H:H	Now--odd as you may think it--it came to pass that this good woman"s wish was fulfilled, just in the very way she had wished it; for, not long afterwards, she had a little boy, who was quite healthy and strong, but was not much bigger than my thumb.
5:5	H:H	H:H	So they said, "Well, we cannot say we have not got what we wished for, and, little as he is, we will love him dearly."
6:6	N:N	H:H	And they called him Thomas Thumb.
7:7	N:Su-	N:H	They gave him plenty of food, yet for all they could do he never grew bigger, but kept just the same size as he had been when he was born.
8:8	N:H	H:H	Still, his eyes were sharp and sparkling, and he soon showed himself to be a clever little fellow, who always knew well what he was about.
9:9	N:A	N:N	One day, as the woodman was getting ready to go into the wood to cut fuel, he said, "I wish I had someone to bring the cart after me, for I want to make haste."
10:10	Su+:N	N:N	"Oh, father," cried Tom, "I will take care of that; the cart shall be in the wood by the time you want it."
11:11	H:Su-	N:N	Then the woodman laughed, and said, "How can that be? you cannot reach up to the horse"s bridle."
12:12	H:H	N:+	"Never mind that, father," said Tom; "if my mother will only harness the horse, I will get into his ear and tell him which way to go."
13:13	N:N	N:+	"Well," said the father, "we will try for once."
14:14	Su+:Su+	+:+	When the time came the mother harnessed the horse to the cart, and put Tom into his ear; and as he sat there the little man told the beast how to go, crying out, "Go on!" and "Stop!" as he wanted: and thus the horse went on just as well as if the woodman had driven it himself into the wood.
15:15	N:N	N:N	It happened that as the horse was going a little too fast, and Tom was calling out, "Gently! gently!" two strangers came up.
16:16	Su-:Su-	-:N	"What an odd thing that is!" said one: "there is a cart going along, and I hear a carter talking to the horse, but yet I can see no one."
17:17	Su-:Su-	-:N	"That is queer, indeed," said the other; "let us follow the cart, and see where it goes."
18:18	N:N	N:N	So they went on into the wood, till at last they came to the place where the woodman was.
19:19	H:H	H:N	Then Tom Thumb, seeing his father, cried out, "See, father, here I am with the cart, all right and safe! now take me down!"
20:20	N:H	H:N	So his father took hold of the horse with one hand, and with the other took his son out of the horse"s ear, and put him down upon a straw, where he sat as merry as you please.
21:21	N:Su-	N:N	The two strangers were all this time looking on, and did not know what to say for wonder.
22:22	Su+:H	D:-	At last one took the other aside, and said, "That little urchin will make our fortune, if we can get him, and carry him about from town to town as a show; we must buy him."
23:23	N:N	N:-	So they went up to the woodman, and asked him what he would take for the little man.
24:24	N:N	D:-	"He will be better off," said they, "with us than with you."
25:25	D:A	D:F	"I won"t sell him at all," said the father; "my own flesh and blood is dearer to me than all the silver and gold in the world."
26:26	H:H	N:F	But Tom, hearing of the bargain they wanted to make, crept up his father"s coat to his shoulder and whispered in his ear, "Take the money, father, and let them have me; I"ll soon come back to you."
27:27	N:N	N:F	So the woodman at last said he would sell Tom to the strangers for a large piece of gold, and they paid the price.
28:28	N:N	N:F	"Where would you like to sit?" said one of them.
29:29	H:H	N:F	"Oh, put me on the rim of your hat; that will be a nice gallery for me; I can walk about there and see the country as we go along."
30:30	N:Sa	N:S	So they did as he wished; and when Tom had taken leave of his father they took him away with them.
31:31	N:N	N:S	They journeyed on till it began to be dusky, and then the little man said, "Let me get down, I"m tired."
32:32	N:N	N:N	So the man took off his hat, and put him down on a clod of earth, in a ploughed field by the side of the road.
33:33	F:N	F:H	But Tom ran about amongst the furrows, and at last slipped into an old mouse-hole.
34:34	H:H	H:H	"Good night, my masters!" said he, "I"m off! mind and look sharp after me the next time."
35:35	A:A	A:H	Then they ran at once to the place, and poked the ends of their sticks into the mouse-hole, but all in vain; Tom only crawled farther and farther in; and at last it became quite dark, so that they were forced to go their way without their prize, as sulky as could be.
36:36	N:N	N:H	When Tom found they were gone, he came out of his hiding-place.
37:37	Su-:F	N:F	"What dangerous walking it is," said he, "in this ploughed field!
38:38	F:F	N:F	If I were to fall from one of these great clods, I should undoubtedly break my neck."
39:39	Su+:H	+:F	At last, by good luck, he found a large empty snail-shell.
40:40	H:H	H:+	"This is lucky," said he, "I can sleep here very well"; and in he crept.
41:41	N:N	F:N	Just as he was falling asleep, he heard two men passing by, chatting together; and one said to the other, "How can we rob that rich parson"s house of his silver and gold?"
42:42	H:N	N:N	"I"ll tell you!" cried Tom.
43:43	F:F	N:N	"What noise was that?" said the thief, frightened; "I"m sure I heard someone speak."
44:44	F:N	N:N	They stood still listening, and Tom said, "Take me with you, and I"ll soon show you how to get the parson"s money."
45:45	N:Su-	N:N	"But where are you?" said they.
46:46	N:N	N:N	"Look about on the ground," answered he, "and listen where the sound comes from."
47:47	N:Su+	N:N	At last the thieves found him out, and lifted him up in their hands.
48:48	Su-:A	N:F	"You little urchin!" they said, "what can you do for us?"
49:49	H:H	N:F	"Why, I can get between the iron window-bars of the parson"s house, and throw you out whatever you want."
50:50	Su+:H	N:F	"That"s a good thought," said the thieves; "come along, we shall see what you can do."
51:51	N:N	N:N	When they came to the parson"s house, Tom slipped through the windowbars into the room, and then called out as loud as he could bawl, "Will you have all that is here?"

53:53	F:F	F:N	Speak low, that you may not awaken anybody."

56:56	F:Su-	F:N	Now the cook lay in the next room; and hearing a noise she raised herself up in her bed and listened.

60:60	A:Su-	A:N	The cook heard this quite plain, so she sprang out of bed, and ran to open the door.
61:61	F:F	N:N	The thieves ran off as if a wolf was at their tails: and the maid, having groped about and found nothing, went away for a light.
62:62	Su+:Su-	N:H	By the time she came back, Tom had slipped off into the barn; and when she had looked about and searched every hole and corner, and found nobody, she went to bed, thinking she must have been dreaming with her eyes open.
63:63	N:N	N:H	The little man crawled about in the hay-loft, and at last found a snug place to finish his night"s rest in; so he laid himself down, meaning to sleep till daylight, and then find his way home to his father and mother.
64:64	F:Sa	F:F	But alas! how woefully he was undone! what crosses and sorrows happen to us all in this world!
65:65	N:Su-	F:-	The cook got up early, before daybreak, to feed the cows; and going straight to the hay-loft, carried away a large bundle of hay, with the little man in the middle of it, fast asleep.
66:66	Su-:Su-	-:-	He still, however, slept on, and did not awake till he found himself in the mouth of the cow; for the cook had put the hay into the cow"s rick, and the cow had taken Tom up in a mouthful of it.
67:67	N:Su-	N:-	"Good lack-a-day!" said he, "how came I to tumble into the mill?"
68:68	F:F	F:F	But he soon found out where he really was; and was forced to have all his wits about him, that he might not get between the cow"s teeth, and so be crushed to death.
69:69	N:N	F:F	At last down he went into her stomach.
70:70	F:F	F:F	"It is rather dark," said he; "they forgot to build windows in this room to let the sun in; a candle would be no bad thing."
71:71	N:Su-	F:F	Though he made the best of his bad luck, he did not like his quarters at all; and the worst of it was, that more and more hay was always coming down, and the space left for him became smaller and smaller.
72:72	F:F	N:F	At last he cried out as loud as he could, "Don"t bring me any more hay!
73:73	N:F	N:F	Don"t bring me any more hay!"
74:74	F:F	N:F	The maid happened to be just then milking the cow; and hearing someone speak, but seeing nobody, and yet being quite sure it was the same voice that she had heard in the night, she was so frightened that she fell off her stool, and overset the milk-pail.

89:89	H:H	H:+	This was just what Tom had reckoned upon; and now he began to set up a great shout, making all the noise he could.

92:92	F:F	N:+	The woodman and his wife, being awakened by the noise, peeped through a crack in the door; but when they saw a wolf was there, you may well suppose that they were sadly frightened; and the woodman ran for his axe, and gave his wife a scythe.

98:98	H:H	H:H	"Ah!" said the father, "what fears we have had for you!"
99:99	H:H	H:H	"Yes, father," answered he; "I have travelled all over the world, I think, in one way or other, since we parted; and now I am very glad to come home and get fresh air again."
100:100	Su-:Su-	N:H	"Why, where have you been?" said his father.
101:101	N:H	H:H	"I have been in a mouse-hole--and in a snail-shell--and down a cow"s throat-- and in the wolf"s belly; and yet here I am again, safe and sound."
102:102	H:H	H:H	"Well," said they, "you are come back, and we will not sell you again for all the riches in the world."
103:103	H:H	H:H	Then they hugged and kissed their dear little son, and gave him plenty to eat and drink, for he was very hungry; and then they fetched new clothes for him, for his old ones had been quite spoiled on his journey.
104:104	H:H	H:H	So Master Thumb stayed at home with his father and mother, in peace; for though he had been so great a traveller, and had done and seen so many fine things, and was fond enough of telling the whole story, he always agreed that, after all, there"s no place like HOME!
